She told CNN in her first nationally televised\ninterview on Tuesday that she was "very disappointed" with Donald Trump for\nhis controversial comments about Mexicans, as well as with the rest of the\nRepublican field for not thoroughly condemning them.\n\n\n\n*The Clinton Campaign Corralled Reporters, But Don\'t Expect Them To Skip\nThe Next Parade\n<http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2015/07/07/hillary-clinton-reporters-parade_n_7746106.html>\n// HuffPo // Michael Calderone \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015 *\n\n\n\nWhen a top-tier presidential candidate strides down Main Street, smiling\nand waving at parade-goers, political reporters and photographers are sure\nto follow. And follow they did on Saturday in Gorham, New Hampshire.\n\n\n\nBut run-of-the-mill campaigning quickly turned into media outrage as aides\nto Hillary Clinton reined in journalists with rope. The literal corralling\nspurred heaps of criticism, both of Clinton\'s campaign for being too\ncontrolling and the press corps for being too subservient.\n\n\n\nThe incident also prompted a larger question for newsrooms grappling with\nso many candidates to cover and so much information to sift through this\nelection cycle: Is sending a reporter to a typical Fourth of July parade\nworth the hassle?\n\n\n\nDaily Beast executive editor Noah Shachtman, who criticized the Clinton\ncampaign\xe2\x80\x99s heavy-handed tactics, said the incident affirmed his site\xe2\x80\x99s\ntactical approach to the crowded field. "We have a small staff, so we\xe2\x80\x99re\nnot going to cover every burp and gaffe of all 900 people running for\npresident," said Shachtman, who did not send a reporter to the parade.\n\n\n\nHe suggested reporters\' time is better spent digging through documents and\nspeaking to people impacted by political leaders\' decisions, rather than\nflocking to stage-managed events. \xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t want to do chock-a-block\ncommodity news," he added, "and so, frankly, these events like parades are\nmost of the time so newsless they\xe2\x80\x99re not even worth covering.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nBut it\'s the possibility of news being made that helps other editors\njustify dispatching reporters to the front lines of flag-strewn parade\nroutes or meet-and-greets at the local diner. A candidate sticking to\ntalking points at one event may open up at the next to a voter, or critic,\nthus offering a unique glimpse into his or her candidacy.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe travel with candidates to see them in action, to see them interact with\nvoters, to see who (and how many people) turn out for their events and to\ngather voters\' impressions,\xe2\x80\x9d Carolyn Ryan, Washington bureau chief for The\nNew York Times, said in an email.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll these moments are revealing, in their own way: You never know when a\ncollege student is going to press Jeb Bush about Iraq or when Martin\nO\'Malley is going to have to talk directly to people upset about the\nviolence in Baltimore,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ryan. She also singled out Okie Howe, a\n98-year-old Army veteran quoted in the paper\xe2\x80\x99s round-up of July 4 campaign\nevents around New Hampshire, who spoke about being a Democrat who was "sick\nof Hillary Clinton" but would likely vote for her anyway.\n\n\n\nThe goal, in essence, is to ensure that you\'re present when a\nnon-manufactured campaign event takes place, even if it means suffering the\noccasional humiliation of a human corralling.\n\n\n\nBuzzFeed political editor Katherine Miller said that while covering 2016\ncandidates, especially Clinton, \xe2\x80\x9cyou don\xe2\x80\x99t have a lot of unpredictable\nmoments or situations where you see them interact with a lot of unknowns.\xe2\x80\x9d\nClinton, she noted, was only at the July 4 parade \xe2\x80\x9cfor 20 minutes and a ton\nof stuff happened.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nThe resulting dispatch, from reporter Ruby Cramer, \xe2\x80\x9cshowed what it looks\nlike in a controlled environment when Clinton is speaking versus a very\nuncontrolled environment and what it says about her, what it says about the\ncampaign," Miller said.\n\n\n\nThat report doesn\'t focus on policy. But for the past three months, much of\nthe story surrounding Clinton has been process-themed -- from her handling\nof emails while secretary of State and the company she keeps to the\ncombustible relationship she has with the press corps.\n\n\n\nClinton, the Democratic front-runner long before she officially jumped in\nthe race in April, essentially has a mini-White House press corps along for\nthe ride. Candidates trying to bolster their name recognition would kill\nfor such attention, but the Clinton team, managing the movements of one of\nthe most famous people in the world, is more likely to bristle at\nreporters\xe2\x80\x99 desire for increased access to the candidate.\n\n\n\nBut so far, disputes over access haven\xe2\x80\x99t led news organizations to stop\ncovering Clinton\'s every move. And neither The New York Times nor BuzzFeed,\npart of a 14-member traveling press pool covering Clinton, is altering\ntheir game plan for dispatching reporters in light of Saturday\xe2\x80\x99s debacle.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think the rope line was bad optics but a bit overblown on Twitter,\xe2\x80\x9d Ryan\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cI do wish Mrs. Clinton had taken some time to answer more reporters\'\nquestions before or after the parade.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n*Hillary Clinton: \xe2\x80\x98People Should and Do Trust Me\xe2\x80\x99\n<http://www.cnn.com/2015/07/07/politics/hillary-clinton-iowa-first-interview/>\n// CNN // Eric Bradner \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015*\n\n\n\nIn Hillary Clinton\'s first national interview of the 2016 race, she\nattacked her Republican rivals on immigration and dismissed the suggestion\nthat the American people have a problem trusting her.\n\n\n\n"People should and do trust me," she told CNN\'s Brianna Keilar.\n\n\n\nShe blamed the "barrage of attacks that are largely fomented by and coming\nfrom the right" for fueling a perception that trust is an area of\nvulnerability for her.\n\n\n\nClinton displayed little hesitation about attacking Republicans herself,\nsaying that she is "very disappointed" in Donald Trump for his comments\nabout immigrants and in the Republican Party for not condemning his remarks\nmore quickly.\n\n\n\nShe then pivoted to skewering the entire GOP field for their immigration\nstance, saying, "They\'re on a spectrum of hostility, which I think is\nreally regrettable in a nation of immigrants like ours."\n\n\n\nThe interview foreshadowed the Clinton that will hit the campaign trail in\nthe coming months as election season heats up. She was occasionally\ndefensive, especially when pressed on whether she has any responsibility\nfor the public\'s mistrust in her. But she had no problem with going on the\noffense against her Republican challengers.\n\n\n\nClinton took direct aim at GOP rival Jeb Bush.\n\n\n\n"He doesn\'t believe in a path to citizenship. If he did at one time, he no\nlonger does," she said.\n\n\n\nThe Bush campaign rebuffed Clinton\'s criticism and instead accused her of\nflip-flopping on immigration.\n\n\n\n"Hillary Clinton will say anything to get elected and her numerous\nflip-flops on immigration prove it," said Emily Benavides, a Bush campaign\nspokeswoman, in a statement.\n\n\n\n"As he outlined in his book on this issue, Gov. Bush believes in a\nconservative legislative solution to fix our broken immigration system that\nincludes earned legal status for those currently in the country after they\npay fines and taxes, learn English and commit no substantial crimes while\nsecuring our border," she said.\n\n\n\nClinton also blamed the city of San Francisco for mishandling the case of\nan undocumented immigrant who had been deported five times before killing a\nwoman there -- in a sanctuary city where local law enforcement do not\nenforce federal immigration laws.\n\n\n\n"The city made a mistake, not to deport someone that the federal government\nstrongly felt should be deported," she said. "I have absolutely no support\nfor a city that ignores the strong evidence that should be acted on."\n\n\n\nThe full interview aired Tuesday on "The Situation Room with Wolf Blitzer"\nand will re-air at 8 p.m. on "Anderson Cooper 360."\n\n\n\nPressed on why the public has a hard time trusting her, Clinton maintained\nshe faced "the same kind of onslaught" in her two New York Senate campaigns\nand her confirmation as secretary of state, and said Republicans have\nsought to turn controversies like her use of a private email address and\nthe Clinton Foundation\'s actions against her.\n\n\n\nAnd that, Clinton said, is why national polls and swing-state surveys have\nfound that a majority of voters say they don\'t see her as honest and\ntrustworthy.\n\n\n\nA Quinnipiac University Swing State poll found that by margins of 8 to 14\npercentage points voters in Florida, Ohio and Pennsylvania are skeptical of\nClinton\'s trustworthiness.\n\n\n\nIn Florida, 51% of voters hold the negative view of Clinton, compared to\n43% who feel she is trustworthy. In Ohio, 53% of voters find Clinton not\ntrustworthy, compared to 40% who do. And in Pennsylvania, 54% of voters\ndon\'t find her honest, while 40% do.\n\n\n\n"I think it\'s understandable that when questions are raised, people maybe\nare thinking about them and wondering about them," Clinton said.\n\n\n\n"But I have every confidence that during the course of this campaign,\npeople are going to know who will fight for them, who will be there when\nthey need them, and that\'s the kind of person I am and that\'s what I will\ndo, not only in a campaign but as president," she said.\n\n\n\nAsked whether she played a role in the sentiment reflected in polls that\nshe\'s not trustworthy, Clinton said: "This has been a theme that has been\nused against me and my husband for many, many years. And at the end of the\nday, I think voters sort it all out."\n\n\n\nShe similarly dismissed questions about her use of a personal email address\non a private server while serving as secretary of state.\n\n\n\nClinton said she turned over all the emails -- including some which show\nher using a secured fax machine, or asking for iced tea during meetings --\nthat had anything to do with public business, and that she broke no laws in\nsticking with one device because she\'s not technically savvy.\n\n\n\n"This is being blown up with no basis in law or in fact. That\'s fine, I get\nit -- this is being, in effect, used by the Republicans in the Congress.\nOK," Clinton said. "But I want people to understand what the truth is, and\nthe truth is, everything I did was permitted and I went above and beyond\nwhat was expected."\n\n\n\nIf elected president, Clinton said she doesn\'t have any plans to push for\nthe closure of the Clinton Foundation -- which has faced criticism for\naccepting foreign contributions during her tenure as America\'s top diplomat\n-- if she\'s elected president.\n\n\n\n"I have no plans to say or do anything about the Clinton foundation other\nthan to say I am proud of it and I think for the good of the world, its\nwork should continue," she said.\n\n\n\nThe interview spanned a broad range of topics -- among them a discussion of\nadding a woman to the $10 bill. Clinton declined to select a favorite woman\nfor the honor, but suggested a woman should instead go on a $20.\n\n\n\n"I don\'t like the idea that as a compromise you would basically have two\npeople on the same bill. One would be a woman. That sounds pretty second\nclass to me," Clinton said. "So I think a woman should have her own bill."\n\n\n\nNor would she pick which Saturday Night Live actress, Amy Poehler or Kate\nMcKinnon, plays the best Hillary Clinton.\n\n\n\n"Amy\'s a friend of mine and Kate\'s doing a great job. You\'re not going to\nget me to pick one or the other," she said. "I think I\'m the best Hillary\nClinton."\n\n\n\n*Top 8 takeaways from Hillary Clinton\'s first big interview\n<http://www.cnn.com/2015/07/07/politics/hillary-clinton-cnn-interview/> //\nCNN // Eric Bradner - July 7, 2015*\n\n\n\nIowa City, Iowa - Hillary Clinton was keenly aware of how long she\'d gone\nas a presidential candidate without sitting down for a nationally televised\ninterview.\n\n\n\n"Ninety days on Sunday," she told CNN\'s Brianna Keilar on Tuesday in the\nfirst of what will be a series of national interviews.\n\n\n\nAs Bernie Sanders rises in the polls among liberals and Republicans\ncontinue to make Clinton the subject of a barrage of attacks, her answers\noffered some insight into the Democratic frontrunner\'s thinking at this\nstage of the campaign.\n\n\n\nHere are eight of Clinton\'s key statements -- and why they matter for 2016:\n\n\n\n1. "This has been a theme that has been used against me and my husband for\nmany, many years."\n\n\n\nThree decades in the limelight have meant both Bill and Hillary Clinton\nhave weathered intense scrutiny and criticism. Clinton acknowledged that\npolls show a majority of Americans don\'t view her as honest and trustworthy\n-- but true to her long-standing posture declined to take any\nresponsibility for the sentiment.\n\n\n\nThe latest round of public incredulity focuses on her use of a private\nemail address while serving as secretary of state, and her family\nfoundation\'s acceptance of foreign contributions during that tenure.\n\n\n\nClinton sees those as the latest in a long line of unfair attacks from\nRepublicans, rather than legitimate lines of inquiry. And she claims she\'s\nnot sweating the "trust" problem.\n\n\n\n"At the end of the day, I think voters sort it all out," Clinton said.\n\n\n\n2. "They\'re on a spectrum of hostility, which I think it\'s really\nregrettable in a nation of immigrants like ours."\n\n\n\nIt was a direct assault on the entire Republican presidential field -- and\nJeb Bush, in particular.\n\n\n\nClinton used a question about Donald Trump\'s controversial remarks about\nMexican immigrants to hammer her GOP opponents, saying that they don\'t\nsupport providing undocumented immigrants with pathways to gaining U.S.\ncitizenship. By painting Bush, Trump and the rest of the Republican field\nwith the same brush, Clinton hopes to hurt the entire party in a general\nelection with Latino voters.\n\n\n\n"I\'m very disappointed in those comments and I feel very bad and very\ndisappointed with him and with the Republican Party for not responding\nimmediately and saying, \'Enough, stop it,\'" Clinton said.\n\n\n\nBush -- and other Republicans -- eventually criticized Trump\'s comments,\nbut only after several days had passed.\n\n\n\n3. "The city made a mistake not to deport someone that the federal\ngovernment strongly felt should be deported."\n\n\n\nClinton still senses some danger on the issue of illegal immigration,\ncriticizing San Francisco for failing to deport a man who\'d been sent back\nto Mexico five times before -- and has admitted killing a woman after\nreturning again.\n\n\n\nThe man was released from police custody in April after drug charges were\ndropped, and he wasn\'t deported because San Francisco is a "sanctuary city"\nfor undocumented immigrants, despite the Department of Homeland Security\nweighing in.\n\n\n\nShe\'s said she supports going further than President Barack Obama has on\nundocumented immigrants -- but only for those who obey the law.\n\n\n\n"I have absolutely no support for a city that ignores the strong evidence\nthat should be acted on," she said.\n\n\n\n4. "I am happy to have a chance to get out and run my campaign as I see fit\nand let other candidates do exactly the same."\n\n\n\nIn other words: I\'m not going to talk about Bernie Sanders.\n\n\n\nRecent polls show Sanders climbing closer to Clinton in New Hampshire and\nIowa, two key early voting states. But Clinton isn\'t yet ready to directly\nengage Sanders, training her fire instead on Republicans.\n\n\n\nThat\'s in part because her campaign wants to see whether Sanders has\nstaying power, and in part because by attacking him, she risks elevating\nhis already growing stature.\n\n\n\n5. "One of the things I learned last time is organize, organize, organize."\n\n\n\nClinton is trying to atone for the sins of her 2008 campaign among Iowa\nDemocratic caucus voters by taking the process seriously.\n\n\n\nIt\'s why she was in Iowa City -- in the heart of eastern Iowa, which went\nheavily for Barack Obama in the 2008 caucuses.\n\n\n\nClinton has shown she\'s able to learn from her mistakes: Those who followed\nher 2008 Iowa campaign say they see a looser, more comfortable candidate;\nand she\'s ejected many of the most divisive figures from that previous bid.\n\n\n\nNow, she\'s touting having signed up a volunteer to work the caucus process\nfor her in all of Iowa\'s more than 1,600 precincts.\n\n\n\n"You\'ve got to get people committed, and then they will bring more people\nthrough," Clinton said.\n\n\n\n6. "I\'m not running my campaign for the press. I\'m running it for the\nvoters."\n\n\n\nThroughout her nascent campaign, Clinton has been criticized for rarely\ntaking questions from the press. That criticism was thrust to the forefront\nagain over the Fourth of July holiday when photos of reporters being held\nback by a moving rope line at a parade were published.\n\n\n\nInstead, Clinton said she\'s preferred small group events that have allowed\nher to reconnect with average Americans after more than six years out of\ndomestic politics. She\'s also been a tireless fundraiser, attending house\nparty after house party to raise $45 million in the campaign\'s first three\nmonths.\n\n\n\nStill, Clinton said she\'s ready to shift gears: The CNN interview, she\nsaid, was the first of what will be a number of sit-downs with national\noutlets.\n\n\n\n7. "A secure fax machine, which is harder to work than the regular."\n\n\n\nA tense exchange about Clinton\'s use of a personal email address on a\nprivate server while at the State Department turned light, when Clinton\ncracked jokes and once again sought to send the message: Nothing to see\nhere, let\'s move along.\n\n\n\n"Now, I didn\'t have to turn over anything," Clinton said. "I chose to turn\nover 55,000 pages because I wanted to go above and beyond what was expected\nof me because I knew the vast majority of everything that was official\nalready was in the State Department system."\n\n\n\nThen, she added: "Now I think it\'s kind of fun. People get to see a\nreal-time, behind-the-scenes look at what I was emailing about and what I\nwas communicating about."\n\n\n\nKeilar highlighted the emails that showed Clinton messaging now-top aide\nJohn Podesta about wearing socks to bed, and an exchange depicting an epic\nstruggle by Clinton to operate a fax machine.\n\n\n\nOn the latter, Clinton couldn\'t help but offer a defense.\n\n\n\n"Yes, a secure fax machine, which is harder to work than the regular," she\nsaid.\n\n\n\n8. "I think I\'m the best Hillary Clinton."\n\n\n\nAsked by Keilar which "Saturday Night Live" actress best satirizes her on\nthe NBC program, Clinton demurred, but offered praise for both Amy Poehler,\nwho impersonated her in 2008, and Kate McKinnon, who has done the job so\nfar in this campaign cycle.\n\n\n\n"Amy\'s a friend of mine and Kate\'s doing a great job. You\'re not going to\nget me to pick one or the other," she said. "I think I\'m the best Hillary\nClinton, to be honest."\n\n\n\n*Hillary Clinton Tweets & Deletes This Photo Of Her Shaking Hands With A\nWhite Man With A "White" Tattoo\n<http://www.bustle.com/articles/95687-hillary-clinton-tweets-deletes-this-photo-of-her-shaking-hands-with-a-white-man-with>\n// Bustle // Melissah Yang \xe2\x80\x93 July 8, 2015*\n\n\n\nHillary Clinton is making the rounds in every corner of the good ol\xe2\x80\x99 U.S.\nof A., and one of her most recent stops was at Lebanon, New Hampshire,\nwhere she encountered all sorts of characters during her campaign trip. The case has\ngained widespread attention from immigration-reform advocates, who say\nVillatoro, 41, was an upstanding member of the community unjustly ripped\naway from his family.\n\n\n\nClinton agreed with a Villatoro supporter, who said federal authorities\nshould have used their discretion not to deport him. She noted that the\ndeportation was based on a misdemeanor charge from the 1990s. "He was from\nall accounts, everything I\'ve read and heard, a contributing member of the\ncommunity. And for the life of me, I don\'t understand why he was deported,"\nshe said. "And I would think we would have to take a hard look at cases\nlike that and exercise more discretion."\n\n\n\nShe credited the Obama administration with making progress on the issue,\nbut said she would push for more movement toward reforming the country\'s\nbroken immigration system. "I still think we should deport dangerous\npeople, dangerous criminals \xe2\x80\x94 they should be gone," she said. "But for\npeople who have maybe one small blemish on their record, and they\'ve proven\nover the years since that they are contributing citizens, I think we should\nshow them understanding and permit them and their families to stay\ntogether."\n\n\n\nFederal authorities have defended Villatoro\'s deportation, saying it was a\nlegitimate part of a sweep of unauthorized immigrants who\'d committed\ncrimes. His lawyer has said the charge stemmed from an attempt to gain a\ndriver\'s license.\n\n\n\nClinton\'s remarks on the case came in response to a question from Aliese\nGingerich, 22, of Iowa City. Gingerich said afterward that she has been\nworking on immigration advocacy in Iowa and when she went to college in\nPennsylvania.\n\n\n\nShe was glad to hear Clinton address the issue. "I think it\'s heartening\nthat she is saying that she wants to pass comprehensive immigration reform\nand work hard with bipartisan efforts that she cited, and things she\'s done\nin the past. I would really like to hear a little bit more specifically\nwhat she wants to pass exactly."\n\n\n\nThe Iowa City stop was one of two Clinton scheduled in Iowa Tuesday on her\nfourth trip to the state since she announced her candidacy in April. It was\nonly the second event so far that was open to the general public, and it\nwas the first one at which she took questions from unscreened voters.\n\n\n\nThe audience questions focused on issues such as the use of military force,\nhow to expand and protect the Affordable Care Act and how to improve\neducation. On the education question, Clinton stressed that she wants the\ncountry to get away from over-reliance on standardized tests and uniform\ninstruction methods. "We have a broad range of kids. We can\'t teach them\nall the same way," she said.\n\n\n\nMore than 350 people got into Clinton\'s event Tuesday at the Iowa City\npublic library, but scores more failed to get in because of a lack of space.\n\n\n\nIowa City is known as the most liberal spot in Iowa. That could make it\nprime territory for Vermont Sen. Bernie Sanders, a populist who is mounting\na surprisingly strong Democratic challenge on Clinton\'s left flank. He has\nbeen rising in the polls and drawing crowds in the thousands in Iowa and\nother states.\n\n\n\nClinton told reporters after her event Tuesday that she was not surprised\nto see Sanders have some success.\n\n\n\n"This is going to be competitive, and it should be competitive," she said.\n"It\'s only the presidency of the United States we\'re talking about. So, you\nknow, the more the better. Let\'s get everybody out there, let\'s get\neverybody working hard, running their own campaigns and then we\'ll leave it\nup to Iowa to decide what happens."\n\n\n\nSeveral voters who came to Clinton\'s Iowa City event said they like what\nSanders says, especially on the issue of income inequality. But they also\nare skeptical of his ability to win the general election.\n\n\n\n"In a perfect world, yes, I would support him, but I\'m not going to throw\nmy vote away," said Tom Braverman, 51, of Iowa City, who is a\nspecial-education teacher. He said that if Democrats nominated Sanders,\nthey would be making the same kind of mistake Republicans will make if they\nnominate a far-right candidate.\n\n\n\nThe library meeting room the campaign chose for the event only held a few\nhundred people. Far more than that came to the library Tuesday morning,\nhoping to see Clinton. At one point, the line outside stretched more than\ntwo blocks.\n\n\n\nUndecided voter Shawn Harmsen, 42, of Iowa City, said he had hoped to ask\nClinton a question in person. He stood about two hours in line, but was\nmore than a third of the way back. "For the people who get in, it\'ll be\ngreat," he said of the relatively small venue. "But for those who don\'t get\nin \xe2\x80\x94 will they come back next time?"\n\n\n\nPeg Voelker, 53, of Iowa City, stood about two-thirds of the way back in\nline. About a half hour before the event started, she said she realized she\nprobably wouldn\'t get in to see Clinton. "But we\'re glad to stand in line\nto show our support," she said. Even if she didn\'t get in this time, she\nsaid, she would be back for future Clinton events and would volunteer for\nthe candidate.\n\n\n\nVoelker, who is self-employed, said she supported Democratic rival John\nEdwards when Clinton ran for the nomination in 2008. There\nare times when it\xe2\x80\x99s hard.\'\n\n\n\nSources who spoke to the Times said that Roger and his family have long\nbenefited from his big brother\'s monetary success in other ways, though.\n\n\n\nBill Clinton reportedly paid for Roger\'s grown-up daughter to attend\ncosmetology school.\n\n\n\nAnd, after leaving the White House, when he worked at investment firm\nYucaipa, Bill Clinton is said by a former employee to have regularly\nwritten his brother checks.\n\nOn another occasion, Roger\'s lawyer floated paying out a settlement over a\ndomestic dispute involving two women on Clinton\'s property with Bill\'s\nmoney.\n\n\n\nBill and Hillary Clinton\'s siblings have been accused on numerous occasions\nof trying to profit off of their relationships with the political power\ncouple, and usually inappropriately.\n\n\n\nHillary\'s older brother Hugh pocketed $400,000 from a Democratic Party\ndonor - In that capacity,\nfellows performed almost identical duties to field organizers, who do the\nmost basic work on a campaign: recruiting volunteers and endorsements,\ngoing door-to-door and calling voters, often outside the confines of a\n9-to-5 day job.\n\n\n\nNow, those people will be paid, the Clinton campaign confirmed to the\nGuardian on Tuesday.\n\n\n\nClinton already had a sizeable footprint on the ground in Iowa and launched\nher campaign with roughly 40 staffers in the state, but the new wave of\nhires represents a decisive step by Clinton to build up her organizing\nefforts in the state.\n\n\n\nThe former secretary of state returned to Iowa on Tuesday for the first\ntime since the opening week of her campaign, speaking to supporters and\norganizers in Iowa City ahead of her first sit-down interview since\nannouncing her second run for the White House.\n\n\n\nThe push to woo ardent Iowa Democrats comes amid a surge of progressive\nappeal from Sanders, the Vermont senator who has been surging in recent\npolls and more than doubled his support among Democrats in the state\nbetween May and June, according to a Quinnipiac University\xe2\x80\x99s survey.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are worried about him,\xe2\x80\x9d the Clinton communications director, Jennifer\nPalmieri, said of Sanders on MSNBC on Monday. \xe2\x80\x9cHe will be a serious force\nfor the campaign, and I don\xe2\x80\x99t think that will diminish.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nThe Iowa hires, however, represent the continuation of a trend in\nClintonland that new staffers \xe2\x80\x93 no matter how much political experience\nthey may bring from previous campaigns \xe2\x80\x93 initially must work for free\nbefore getting on the Clinton payroll.\n\n\n\nMany high-ranking staffers worked for free as \xe2\x80\x9cvolunteers\xe2\x80\x9d on the Clinton\ncampaign in the weeks before she formally launched her campaign in early\nApril. Political watchers told the Guardian last month it represented an\nattempt to wiggle around federal campaign-finance rules that ban an\nundeclared campaign from paying staff.\n\n\n\nWhen Federal Election Commission reports are released next week, Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s\ndonations are expected to far exceed any other Democratic candidate.\n\n\n\nClinton\xe2\x80\x99s competitors have already taken jabs at her use of the unpaid\nfellows: Lis Smith, the deputy campaign manager for former Maryland\ngovernor Martin O\xe2\x80\x99Malley, tweeted over the Independence Day weekend that\n\xe2\x80\x9cwe all know she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t pay young staff\xe2\x80\x9d.\n\n\n\nStill, Clinton has been polling well above 50% in the state where she\nfinished third in the 2008 Democratic caucus.\n\n\n\n*Clinton Still Far Outpaces Her Democratic Rivals\n<http://www.rasmussenreports.com/public_content/politics/elections/election_2016/clinton_still_far_outpaces_her_democratic_rivals>\n// Rasmussen Reports \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015 *\n\n\n\nDespite the increasing media coverage going to some of her rivals for the\n2016 Democratic presidential nomination, Hillary Clinton remains hugely\nahead as far as the party\xe2\x80\x99s voters are concerned.\n\n\n\nNinety-three percent (93%) of Likely Democratic Voters believe Clinton is\nlikely to end up being their party\xe2\x80\x99s nominee next year, according to a new\nRasmussen Reports national telephone survey. Bernie Sanders already\nhas\n<http://grist.org/news/will-hillary-clinton-swear-off-fossil-fuel-money-bernie-sanders-already-has/>\n// Grist // John Light \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015 *\n\n\n\nThe Nation magazine and 350 Action are challenging presidential candidates\nto \xe2\x80\x9cneither solicit nor accept campaign contributions\xe2\x80\x9d from fossil fuel\ncompanies \xe2\x80\x94 and that\xe2\x80\x99s putting the heat on Hillary Clinton in particular.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cBack in the 1990s, politicians on both sides of the aisle swore off\ncampaign contributions from big tobacco because the industry lied to the\nAmerican people about the damage it was causing to public health,\xe2\x80\x9d writes\n350 Action spokesperson Jamie Henn in an email. Oil, coal, and gas\ncompanies, Henn continues, \xe2\x80\x9chave consistently misled the public about the\ndangers associated with their product, and this time it\xe2\x80\x99s the whole planet\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s at stake. You can\xe2\x80\x99t be serious about addressing climate change and\nstill accept checks from ExxonMobil.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nFossil fuel companies, of course, exercise quite a bit of influence over\npolitics through their ability to lob money into campaigns. Though coal\ncompanies\xe2\x80\x99 profits have been suffering, oil companies are going strong, and\nboth still put a lot of money into politics. The oil and gas industry\npoured $76 million into federal campaigns in 2012, according to the Center\nfor Responsive Politics. Of the amount that was donated to individual\ncandidates\xe2\x80\x99 campaigns, 89 percent went to Republicans and 11 percent to\nDemocrats. The coal mining industry gave another $15 million in 2012, and\nmost of the candidates it supported were Republicans too.\n\n\n\nSo The Nation\xe2\x80\x99s editors issued a challenge to candidates in an editorial\nyesterday:\n\n\n\nTo break the carbon barons\xe2\x80\x99 grip over America\xe2\x80\x99s response to this crisis,\nThe Nation calls upon all 2016 presidential and congressional candidates to\nmake and honor the following pledge: In the name of protecting our country\nand the world from the growing dangers of climate change, I will neither\nsolicit nor accept campaign contributions from any oil, gas, or coal\ncompany.\n\n\n\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s an interesting experiment, and how candidates choose to respond to it\nsays a lot about their priorities. \xe2\x80\x9cSome candidates for president are\nalready signing up,\xe2\x80\x9d writes Henn, \xe2\x80\x9cand we expect more to do so as campaigns\nlike divestment continue to stigmatize the fossil fuel industry.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nDemocrat Bernie Sanders and the Green Party\xe2\x80\x99s Jill Stein have said they\xe2\x80\x99re\nin \xe2\x80\x94 they\xe2\x80\x99ll take the pledge. Former Maryland governor Martin O\xe2\x80\x99Malley and\nformer Rhode Island governor and U.S. senator Lincoln Chafee \xe2\x80\x94 also both\nrunning as Democrats \xe2\x80\x94 \xe2\x80\x9csaid they supported strong climate action but would\nnot sign the pledge,\xe2\x80\x9d write the magazine\xe2\x80\x99s editors in the editorial.\n[UPDATE, 7/7/15: O\xe2\x80\x99Malley now tells The Nation that he will take the\npledge.] Nation Executive Editor Richard Kim told Grist that they\xe2\x80\x99re\nreaching out to Jim Webb, former senator from Virginia, who jumped into the\nrace as a Democrat last week.\n\n\n\nOn the other side, not one of the 14 Republican candidates The Nation\ncontacted has responded to the challenge. All but one of the Republicans\ndeny that human-made climate change is a real threat that should be taken\nseriously by our next president. The one outlier, Sen. Lindsey Graham\n(S.C.), has said he\xe2\x80\x99s in favor of addressing \xe2\x80\x9cclimate change [and] CO2\nemissions in a business-friendly way,\xe2\x80\x9d but he\xe2\x80\x99s a long-shot candidate. And,\nwell, it honestly doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem too likely that Republicans, whose party has\nembraced the \xe2\x80\x9cmoney is speech\xe2\x80\x9d-type decisions from the Supreme Court that\npaved the way for the current explosion in campaign spending, would say no\non principle to money from a big industry \xe2\x80\x94 especially when the donor\nindustry in question so clearly favors Republicans over their Democratic\nopponents.\n\n\n\nThat puts the pressure from The Nation and 350 Action\xe2\x80\x99s challenge on\nHillary Clinton.\n\n\n\nClinton, who has yet to respond to the challenge, has acknowledged that\nclimate change is a potent threat. Were she to win, she\xe2\x80\x99d be following\nanother Democratic president who, at least on the demand side of things,\nhas pushed to green America\xe2\x80\x99s energy economy, and who has worked\ninternationally to encourage other large polluting countries to do the\nsame. Climate change is an issue she\xe2\x80\x99ll have to engage with continually\nthrough the election cycle, and oil and coal companies\xe2\x80\x99 objectives are,\npresumably, at odds with those of a candidate who has called for \xe2\x80\x9cdecisive\xe2\x80\x9d\naction to \xe2\x80\x9chead off the most catastrophic consequences\xe2\x80\x9d of climate change.\n\n\n\nSo will Clinton heed this latest effort to push her to the left? Her\ncampaign has started to see Sanders as a real threat, and it\xe2\x80\x99s attempting\nto emulate his campaign by courting small donors. Choosing to turn down\nmoney from companies with a vested interest in stopping climate action is\none easy way Clinton could show her commitment to the liberal base.\n\n\n\nThe Nation and 350 Action plan to keep up the pressure, on Clinton and the\nother candidates who haven\xe2\x80\x99t pledged yet. \xe2\x80\x9cAfter all, candidates can change\ntheir minds,\xe2\x80\x9d the Nation editors write, \xe2\x80\x9cespecially when enough public\npressure is brought to bear.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n*Judicial Watch: Federal Judge Orders State Department to Release Documents\nRegarding Hillary Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s iPhone and iPad Use\n<http://www.judicialwatch.org/press-room/press-releases/judicial-watch-federal-judge-orders-state-department-to-release-documents-regarding-hillary-clintons-iphone-and-ipad-use/>\n// Judicial Watch \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015 *\n\n\n\nJudicial Watch announced today that a federal judge has ordered the State\nDepartment to begin releasing documents by August 2015 regarding former\nSecretary of State Hillary Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s requests for use of an iPad and\niPhone.  The order comes in response to a Judicial Watch lawsuit (Judicial\nWatch, Inc. v. U.S. Department of State (No. 1:15-cv-00646)) seeking\ndocuments related to Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s use of an iPhone and iPad to conduct\nofficial business while secretary of state.\n\n\n\nIn her ruling, Judge Colleen Kollar-Kotelly required the State Department\nto produce the first round of relevant documents by August 20, 2015, and\nheld that Judicial Watch and the State Department will be responsible for\nfiling a joint status report by September 1, 2015, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\xa6proposing a schedule\nfor further productions and indicating the volume and scope of responsive\ndocuments.\xe2\x80\x9d  Beginning August 20, the State Department will also be\nrequired to release new documents every six weeks.\n\n\n\nThough the State Department did not object to producing records beginning\nin August, Judge Kollar-Kotelly denied the State Department\xe2\x80\x99s request to\nset January 2016 as the final document production date, holding:\n\n\n\nThe Court will not set a final deadline for all productions until after it\nhas had an opportunity to consider that Joint Status Report\xe2\x80\x94in order to\nleave open the possibility that the production of documents can be\ncompleted more expeditiously than the January 2016 deadline proposed by the\nDefendant.\n\n\n\nIn the most recent report to the court, Judicial Watch opposed the State\nDepartment\xe2\x80\x99s request to extend final production through January 2016.\n\n\n\nJudicial Watch filed the lawsuit after the Department of State failed to\ncomply with Judicial Watch\xe2\x80\x99s March 10, 2015, Freedom of Information Act\n(FOIA) request seeking release of the following:\n\n\n\nAny and all records of requests by former Secretary of State Hillary Rodham\nClinton or her staff to the State Department Office Security Technology\nseeking approval for the use of an iPad or iPhone for official government\nbusiness; and\n\n\n\nAny and all communications within or between the Office of the Secretary of\nState, the Executive Secretariat, and the Office of the Secretary and the\nOffice of Security Technology concerning, regarding, or related to the use\nof unauthorized electronic devices for official government business.\n\n\n\nOn March 31, 2015, The Associated Press reported that Clinton, while\nsecretary of state, had used an iPad to email members of her staff,\ncontradicting her statements that she had used a secret email account so\nthat she could conveniently conduct official business on one electronic\ndevice alone.  Reports of Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s use of both a secret email server based\nat her residence and of an iPad to conduct government business have also\nraised concerns about the security of Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s communications.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe federal courts are doing a significant service to the American people\nby ordering the State Department to release documents regarding Mrs.\nClinton\xe2\x80\x99s iPhone and iPad use,\xe2\x80\x9d said Judicial Watch President Tom Fitton.\n\xe2\x80\x9cRather than admitting that it unlawfully, obstructed the public\xe2\x80\x99s right to\nknow for years and start producing records, the Obama administration has\nstonewalled and fought Judicial Watch in court.
