When one of Green\xe2\x80\x99s prot\xc3\xa9g\xc3\xa9s approached\nan official on the Indiana Republican Party\xe2\x80\x99s 2006 coordinated campaign\nasking if he could integrate experiments into the party\xe2\x80\x99s efforts, there\nwas only one question that mattered: Was the political scientist \xe2\x80\x9cin the\nfamily\xe2\x80\x9d? That described a very specific qualification\xe2\x80\x94having worked on one\nof the two Bush-Cheney campaigns\xe2\x80\x94and it was not on David Nickerson\xe2\x80\x99s CV.\nSoon, though, Nickerson had a Ph.D. from Yale, then an associate\nprofessorship at Notre Dame, and then, in 2012, a title that had never\nbefore existed in the history of presidential politics: director of\nexperiments at Obama for America.\n\n\n\nNickerson\xe2\x80\x99s trajectory confirmed the extent to which Obama-era Democratic\noperatives have scientized the project of electioneering. The party\xe2\x80\x99s\nascendant young operatives\xe2\x80\x94notably Robby Mook, Hillary Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s campaign\nmanager\xe2\x80\x94nearly all had formative experience working on voter contact\n(rather than strategizing communications or managing political\nrelationships) in a period of technical transformation. They were\nconditioned to see every aspect of citizen behavior as potentially\npredictable through statistical modeling, and everything a campaign did to\nshape it as a potentially testable hypothesis. Meanwhile, the fractured\nremnants of the Bush-era Republican Party did not indulge such curiosity\nwithin its ranks, and when it did emerge, did little to channel it\nproductively. \xe2\x80\x9cAfter 2004 the drive just left. It was no longer there\xe2\x80\x94we no\nlonger did the testing and we ended up where we ended up in 2012. We just\nkind of stopped,\xe2\x80\x9d Hazelwood says. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was terribly frustrating after 2012\nto see what happened, and know we needed to get back to testing, and having\nthat housed in one place to help all conservatives.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nHazelwood made the case to Bradshaw that the party should take the\ninitiative in spurring such a cultural shift, albeit with a different\nobjective than the one that had inspired the 72-Hour Task Force tests.\n\xe2\x80\x9cBack then we framed it completely differently. It was, \xe2\x80\x98We\xe2\x80\x99re getting back\nto grassroots and showing why grassroots works,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Hazelwood explains. \xe2\x80\x9cNow\nit\xe2\x80\x99s about tests, and getting to test return on investment and how you\nmeasure efficiency and effectiveness.\xe2\x80\x9d Hazelwood helped Bradshaw rewrite\nthe task force report\'s section on \xe2\x80\x9ccampaign mechanics\xe2\x80\x9d to offer its\nimprimatur for what had become her pet project. Republicans should\n\xe2\x80\x9cidentify a team of strategists and funders to build a data analytics\ninstitute that can capture and distill best practices for communication to\nand targeting of specific voters,\xe2\x80\x9d as the report put it. \xe2\x80\x9cUsing the GOP\xe2\x80\x99s\ndata, the data analytics institute would work to develop a specific set of\ntests for 2013 and 2014\xe2\x80\x94tests on voter registration, persuasion,\nGet-Out-The-Vote, and voter mobilization\xe2\x80\x94that will then be adopted into\nfuture programs to ensure that our voter contact and targeting dollars are\nspent on proven performance.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nThe report cautiously avoided suggesting that the RNC incubate or host such\nan institute itself. The national party was a subject of more distrust\namong conservative activists than it had been during the early Bush years,\nand after Citizens United even more political spending was being driven by\ngroups that were legally forbidden from coordinating with the party or its\ncandidates. Hazelwood found a model for such a \xe2\x80\x9cdata analytics institute\xe2\x80\x9d\nin the organization that had paved Nickerson\xe2\x80\x99s way into Democratic\ncampaigns after he was turned away by Indiana\xe2\x80\x99s Republicans. In 2007,\nsimilarly driven to self-examination by their inability to dislodge an\nincumbent president they assumed was beatable, a group of lefty operatives\nlaunched the Analyst Institute, to serve as a \xe2\x80\x9cclearinghouse for\nevidence-based best practices in progressive voter contact.\xe2\x80\x9d To do so, they\nformalized a series of \xe2\x80\x9cgeek lunches,\xe2\x80\x9d held at the AFL-CIO, at which\nindividual operatives and researchers presented the scattered experiments\nthey had been running on their own, into a stand-alone entity. Since then,\nthe Analyst Institute\xe2\x80\x99s work has transformed the techniques of Democratic\ncampaigners in the Obama era, and eventually contracted to embed its\nanalysts in the president\xe2\x80\x99s re-election effort in 2012. Hazelwood decided\nto structure her project similarly, as a for-profit consulting firm that\ncould operate with little concern for ever turning a profit, a status that\nallows it to work with parties and campaigns as clients without being\nsubject to disclosure laws itself. Hazelwood was transparent in her debt to\nthe Analyst Institute, down to the unhelpfully abstract name.\n\n\n\nShe pitched her new effort to collaborators across the right, eager to take\nadvantage of the oddball calendar of off-year elections to begin running\ntests. There was one in Massachusetts, where the American Crossroads\nsuper-PAC  agreed to randomly assign its robocalls on behalf of Gabriel\nGomez, a first-time candidate challenging Representative Ed Markey to\nreplace John Kerry in the Senate. In Minneapolis\xe2\x80\x99s mayoral campaign that\nsame year, Hazelwood worked with the pro-business Minnesota Jobs Coalition\nto test what type of messaging works best to boost a conservative candidate\nin a officially non-partisan race with a large fragmented field.\n\n\n\nIn parallel, Kirby was also digging more deeply into experiments. He had\nspent 2012 working at FreedomWorks, the well-funded conservative grassroots\norganization that had invested heavily in field activity designed to help\nelect a Republican president and a more conservative Congress. Kirby\nemerged from the experience resentful of the the confidence political\nprofessionals brought to their decisions about tactical approaches. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\nwere disappointed in 2012 with how much money we spent and how little we\nhad to show for it,\xe2\x80\x9d Kirby says. \xe2\x80\x9cI felt integrity-bound to ask whether we\ncould do any better.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nKirby was drawn to the experimental method as a tool for resolving that\ncuriosity. He signed up for a brief summer course on randomized trials\ntaught by Green, the Columbia political scientist recognized as his\ndiscipline\xe2\x80\x99s most prominent evangelist for field experiments as the only\ntool capable of truly disentangling cause and effect in campaigns. (Green\xe2\x80\x99s\nwork recently gained broader attention when he was forced to retract a\npaper he had published in the journal Science, about the effectiveness of\nsending gay canvassers to advocate for same-sex marriage, amid accusations\nthat his co-author Michael LaCour had falsified survey data.) Green was\ninspired by Kirby\xe2\x80\x99s enthusiasm, and\xe2\x80\x94after years of finding growing interest\nin his methods from from labor unions, environmental advocates, and ACORN\n\xe2\x80\x94the opportunity to see if conservative targets responded differently to\npolitical communication than liberal ones. \xe2\x80\x9cAlmost all the research to date\nhas been on the left or center-left. Very little of it has been on the\ncenter-right or right,\xe2\x80\x9d Green says. \xe2\x80\x9cThere isn\xe2\x80\x99t a base of knowledge about\nthe message, the messenger, and the audience they care about.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nKirby was interested in starting with lawn signs. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the most elemental\npolitical tactic,\xe2\x80\x9d he says. \xe2\x80\x9cAt FreedomWorks we spent probably at least a\nmillion dollars on that\xe2\x80\x94hundreds and thousands of yard signs.\xe2\x80\x9d Yard signs\nwere often held up as an example of wasteful and pointless campaign\nspending (\xe2\x80\x9cyard signs don\xe2\x80\x99t vote\xe2\x80\x9d is a frequent refrain of the bien pensant\npolitical class), although there was little empirical evidence to sustain\nthat skepticism. The limited research literature could be attributed partly\nto methodological complications\xe2\x80\x94it is not easy to control for the spillover\neffects of, say, voters who drive past signs in a precinct other than the\none in which they vote\xe2\x80\x94but some stemmed from a lack of obvious incentive\nfor political professionals to run an experiment. \xe2\x80\x9cIn the case of lawn\nsigns, what\xe2\x80\x99s odd is if you\xe2\x80\x99re dealing with a person who is deeply\nskeptical, they\xe2\x80\x99re not interested enough to do a test,\xe2\x80\x9d says Green. \xe2\x80\x9cPeople\nworking in low-salience races see them as their one and only affordable\ntactic and don\xe2\x80\x99t want to take out a control group.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nWorking with Green, Kirby ran an experiment in which Virginia precincts\nwere isolated and then randomly assigned to have signs placed at a certain\ndensity in road medians, each of them photographed and geotagged so it\nwould be possible to monitor whether they were removed by opponents or\nvandals. The signs had little impact on election results in those\nprecincts, they believe\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cmaybe a glimmer of an effect\xe2\x80\x9d on both turnout and\nvote share, according to Green\xe2\x80\x94but given how cheap signs are to print and\nplace, the experimenters thought that they might have demonstrated that\nsigns might not be a total waste after all.\n\n\n\nKirby became an enthusiastic booster of Hazelwood\xe2\x80\x99s project. After he went\nto work at the Cato Institute in early 2014, he offered one of the think\ntank\xe2\x80\x99s large conference rooms to host the right\xe2\x80\x99s version of the \xe2\x80\x9cgeek\nlunch.\xe2\x80\x9d The first CSI meeting, in late 2013, drew more than 100 people from\nacross conservative politics. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was the right moment,\xe2\x80\x9d says Kirby.\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeople were curious.\xe2\x80\x9d Green led a three-hour introduction to the\nexperimental method, and encouraged attendees to think of it as a tool for\nassessing the effectiveness of their current tactics and auditioning new\nones. Many of the experiments that attendees concocted in response\xe2\x80\x94to test\ndirect mail and phone calls in relation to turnout\xe2\x80\x94addressed research\nquestions that some lefty groups felt they had settled through replication\nas much as a decade earlier. \xe2\x80\x9cIt wasn\xe2\x80\x99t breaking any new ground,\xe2\x80\x9d Kirby\nsays, \xe2\x80\x9cbut at least it was getting the muscles flexing.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nWithin the CSI circle, there is resignation that one cultural problem\nRepublicans face modernizing their campaigns remains beyond their immediate\ncontrol: Academic social scientists capable of designing, administering,\nand analyzing field experiments are overwhelmingly on the Democratic side.\nFor his part, Green\xe2\x80\x94with his politically catholic view toward\nexperimentation and promiscuous search for collaborators who will let him\ntest scientific theories in the real world\xe2\x80\x94remained something of a unicorn.\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe left enjoys a lot of sympathetic academics and legions of\nideologically aligned grad students that we on the right just won\'t have\nthe same level of access to,\xe2\x80\x9d says Brian Stobie, a former official at the\nCharles Koch Institute who attempted to recruit academic collaborators to\ntest the Koch network\xe2\x80\x99s election-year operations before leaving to start\nhis own firm. (One political scientist approached for this article did not\nwant to acknowledge on the record any openness of graduate students to\npartner with conservative organizations on research projects, for fear of\ncausing the graduate students reputational damage.)\n\n\n\nThe emergence of the CSI circle has also unmasked a generational divide\nmore fundamental than the one between practitioners of online and offline\npoliticking that defines most press coverage of the Republicans\xe2\x80\x99 technology\npredicament. There is a Web-savvy new guard that chafes against the\nold-line consultants who continue to profit from spending on television,\nphone, and mail. But these disputes\xe2\x80\x94about the percentage of a campaign\nbudget devoted to Web and mobile advertising, or whether a digital\nconsultant has a proverbial \xe2\x80\x9cseat at the table\xe2\x80\x9d on strategic decisions\xe2\x80\x94are\nlost on the CSI crowd, who have a scientific bent. Many there are eager to\ntake on the digital consultants and the grandiose claims some of the\ndigital people make, unchallenged, about their potential impact on\nelections. Those in the experimental set pronounce themselves agnostic\nabout tactics, interested only in those whose worth have been proven,\nempirically. What can be measured to deliver votes\xe2\x80\x94rather than just clicks\nor eyeballs\xe2\x80\x94and at what cost?\n\n\n\nLast May, strategist Dave Carney demonstrated for the CSI seminar what such\nskepticism could look like when put into practice. He and University of\nTexas political scientist Daron Shaw had come to present findings from a\ncomplicated series of experiments that Carney had invited Shaw to conduct\nwithin the gubernatorial campaign of Texas Attorney General Greg Abbott.\n(The two had worked together on Perry\xe2\x80\x99s 2006 re-election, when Shaw and\nGreen served on a team of in-house experimenters that Carney named his\n\xe2\x80\x9ceggheads,\xe2\x80\x9d a collaboration that has produced more peer-reviewed papers\nthan any other campaign in history.) Shaw could have merely presented his\nfindings in the form of charts and graphs, but that would not have fully\nsatisfied Carney\xe2\x80\x99s preference for dramatic colloquy. He invited the\nconsultants and vendors whose services he had audited to be part of the\ndiscussion\xe2\x80\x94an equivalent of beauty-pageant contestants being forced to\nstand on stage while judges render a verdict on their presence and poise.\n\n\n\nShaw had randomized the delivery of $1.6 million worth of campaign\ncommunications\xe2\x80\x94across broadcast and cable television, a variety of digital\nformats, radio, and direct mail\xe2\x80\x94with effects measured at both individual\nand neighborhood units. Three entire media markets, including El Paso and\nAmarillo, were assigned to a control group that received no Abbott campaign\ncommunication over the course of that month. Unlike many studies, Carney\nhad begun by asking Abbott\xe2\x80\x99s consultants to devise plans at a budget that\nwould \xe2\x80\x9callow them to do what they needed to in order to produce an impact.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of the most common criticisms one hears from practitioners about\npolitical science experiments is that the treatment is \xe2\x80\x98not what we\xe2\x80\x99d do in\na real campaign,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d Shaw explained. \xe2\x80\x9cThe obvious solution is to give the\nprofessionals the resources and discretion to design and implement their\npreferred outreach within the context of the experiment.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nComparing polls conducted before and after the experiment, Shaw explained\nto the CSI seminar that three weeks of campaigning increased Abbott\xe2\x80\x99s\npopularity by nearly eight points among the voters he was trying to\npersuade, with targeted broadcast television buys doing so with the\ngreatest force. At the same time, voters in zip codes served Facebook and\nonline pre-roll video ads were less likely to support Abbott afterward, and\nturned out at the same rate as those who lived in areas that never received\nthe ads. (Shaw did find that, in some combinations, Internet advertising\ndid have a modestly positive impact on turnout, according to\nvoter-registration records updated after the primary.) Digital consultants\n\xe2\x80\x9cmight argue that the relatively modest effects associated with online\noutreach in our tests still constitute a more cost-effective investment\nthan broadcast television,\xe2\x80\x9d Shaw acknowledged in an article, which, as part\nof his deal with Abbott\xe2\x80\x99s campaign, he has submitted to political-science\njournals for publication.\n\n\n\nHis presentation of \xe2\x80\x9cThe Abbott Campaign Experiments: Persuasion and\nTurnout in Texas\xe2\x80\x9d wowed the CSI seminar, as much for its ambition and\naudacity as any specific finding. But to the disappointment of many\nattendees, few other consultants expressed an interest in mimicking\nCarney\xe2\x80\x99s adventurousness. \xe2\x80\x9cTesting, whether done on the right or left,\ntends to show that the majority of what you think \xe2\x80\x98works\xe2\x80\x99 doesn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d says\nStobie, who after the 2012 election launched 0ptimus Consulting to conduct\nexperiments with the promise of helping clients to constrain their\nspending. \xe2\x80\x9cTo the majority of operatives and organizations, paying for the\nopportunity to find out much of what you are doing is ineffective is not an\nappealing investment.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nA few weeks ago, Hazelwood met Priebus for lunch at the Capitol Hill Club\nand handed over the results of an experimental study he had commissioned\nfrom her firm. Hazelwood\xe2\x80\x99s reputation had impressed Priebus long before the\ntwo first met, in 2009, back when he was a lawyer who oversaw field offices\nnear his Wisconsin home and had \xe2\x80\x9cdone every yard-sign job in the party you\ncan imagine.\xe2\x80\x9d Sharing a ticket with Bush in 2004 during a failed state\nSenate run, Priebus saw how, by \xe2\x80\x9cdividing states into turf, and measuring\noutcomes and metrics,\xe2\x80\x9d as he puts it, Hazelwood\xe2\x80\x99s internal reforms had\nmodernized the party\xe2\x80\x99s approach to field organizing. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve known her as the\nauthor of that concept,\xe2\x80\x9d Priebus says. \xe2\x80\x9cI really do understand the ground\ngame and what we need to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nThe 72-Hour Task Force had been established to convince local party\nactivists like Priebus that the ground game was not merely something one\ndid or didn\xe2\x80\x99t do\xe2\x80\x94that some tactical approaches to it were superior to\nothers. Hazelwood had isolated \xe2\x80\x9cprecinct organizing\xe2\x80\x9d (assigning a full-time\nfield staffer for a month), \xe2\x80\x9cGOP flushing\xe2\x80\x9d (indiscriminately turning out\nRepublican voters in the last two days before an election), and \xe2\x80\x9cvolunteer\ncalling\xe2\x80\x9d (compared to paid call centers) as three distinct activities, and\nsubjected each to separate tests. As a result of the impact\xe2\x80\x94two to three\npercentage points increase in turnout for each, they found\xe2\x80\x94the Task Force\nprescribed that Republican campaigns assign one person to be individually\nresponsible for every 40 to 100 voters. \xe2\x80\x9cBack to People Power,\xe2\x80\x9d an internal\nRNC presentation titled the recommendation at the time .\n\n\n\nAfter Bush\xe2\x80\x99s re-election, the party let much of that field infrastructure\nwither, along with the ethos behind and intellectual justification for it.\nDuring the years since, experiments have revealed a cognitive logic for\nunderstanding what mobilizes voters. Basic interactions immediately became\nmore potent when they were personalized in a way that introduced a sense of\nindividual responsibility around voting. Campaigns now frequently remind\ncitizens of their past history casting a ballot, and recruit them to sign\npledges to do so again. (One of Green\xe2\x80\x99s most famous tests, conducted with\nhis former Yale colleague Alan Gerber, sent voters a copy of their past\nregistration records with a threat to publicize a set after an election, so\nothers could see whether or not they voted. It proved the most effective\nget-out-the-vote technique ever measured.) The behavioral psychology behind\nsuch techniques gave new depth to campaigns\xe2\x80\x99 understanding of \xe2\x80\x9cpeople\npower.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nHazelwood developed a hypothesis that people power could learn something\nfrom stalking. Campaigns have long thought of voter contact as part of a\nnarrative progression: a flight of six direct-mail pieces over which a\ncandidate\xe2\x80\x99s argument unfurls, or a \xe2\x80\x9clayered\xe2\x80\x9d approach in which phone calls\nand door-knocks are delivered in a deliberately choreographed sequence. But\ncampaigns rarely share these plans with voters, often preferring to let the\ninteractions speak for themselves and not draw undue attention to their\nvolume. Instead, Hazelwood thought it was time to mash up the various\ntactics that the 72-Hour Task Force had segregated into distinct programs.\nJust as the pledges and the vote-history techniques had, walking voters\nthrough the process as it was happening could produce a personal engagement\nwith the election.\n\n\n\nHazelwood was anxious that the party not let a busy midterm election year\npass without using every opportunity to hone tactics it could deploy in\nearnest in 2016. After San Diego announced a special mayoral election,\nscheduled for February 2014, Hazelwood convinced a friend managing\nRepublican Kevin Faulconer\xe2\x80\x99s ultimately succesful campaign to let CSI get\ninvolved. Hazelwood took command of some of the party\xe2\x80\x99s field programs\nthere, randomly assigning some areas to receive specialized attention from\nlocal precinct captains. When she was able to show that her tactics had\nincreased turnout by 8.5 percent among the party\xe2\x80\x99s so-called\n\xe2\x80\x9clow-propensity voters,\xe2\x80\x9d the party\xe2\x80\x99s chairman took notice.\n\n\n\nThat finding intersected neatly with what Priebus had identified as his\nparty\xe2\x80\x99s strategic challenge, and one of the areas the Growth and\nOpportunity Project had blamed for the party\xe2\x80\x99s inability to win the\npresidency or the Senate in 2012. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re a really good midterm party, but\nfor some reason presidential-year-only voters have been trouble for us,\xe2\x80\x9d\nPriebus says. \xe2\x80\x9cIf we put people out in the field\xe2\x80\x94and we have a limited\namount of people and limited amount of time\xe2\x80\x94what\xe2\x80\x99s the combination of\nthings we can do that have the best effect of turning out people who vote\nonly in presidential years?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nOver the summer, Priebus signed off on Hazelwood\xe2\x80\x99s proposal to have CSI\nhelp answer that question. He was insistent that Hazelwood\xe2\x80\x99s experiments\nnot interfere with the party\xe2\x80\x99s plans to spend $105 million across key races\nin mitdterm battlegrounds. In three states, however, Hazelwood\xe2\x80\x99s firm was\npermitted to layer atop the party\xe2\x80\x99s extant organization an additional\nprecinct program that would target the type of low-propensity voters that\nRepublican strategists did not expect to necessarily vote in 2014, but knew\nthey needed to mobilize in 2016. After working with Hazelwood on how to\nstructure her study, Green directed her to enlist Oklahoma State University\nprofessor Brandon Lenoir, whom he had guided on another lawn-sign\nexperiment, for help implementing it. (There is such entrenched skepticism\nabout yard signs that Green has waited to publish until he has five\nexperiments, each conducted in distinct geographical and political\ncontexts, that can be assembled into a single paper with more comprehensive\nfindings than Kirby\xe2\x80\x99s alone.)\n\n\n\nIn nine counties across Colorado, Iowa, and Arkansas, Hazelwood\xe2\x80\x99s team\nworked with the state parties and the Republican Senate candidate to hire\ndedicated local organizers it called \xe2\x80\x9cprecinct captains.\xe2\x80\x9d Each was given a\nlist of voters who had cast a ballot in one or fewer of the past four major\nelections, with directions to visit each voter in mid-October for an\nintroductory conversation. \xe2\x80\x9cI was designated your Precinct Captain for the\nRepublican Party, for the duration of the election,\xe2\x80\x9d the canvassing script\ninstructed. Then, over the next few weeks, the precinct captain would make\ncontact four more times, in a specific order: again at the door, then by\nphone, followed by a postcard, and a then final door visit.  If the voter\nwas home each time, it meant a recurring interaction with an increasingly\nfamiliar neighborhood figure.\n\n\n\nIf the voter didn\xe2\x80\x99t answer the door or phone, however, the precinct captain\nwas instructed to make the missed encounter just as personally memorable.\nEach door hanger left behind should have a handwritten Post-it note\nattached to it, with a message that the \xe2\x80\x9cpersonalized note instructions\xe2\x80\x9d\nrecommended should say: \xe2\x80\x9cHi Mary, sorry I missed you today! \xe2\x80\xa6 I will be\nback in the neighborhood again next week and try to catch you then.\xe2\x80\x9d The\npostcard would pick up the theme: \xe2\x80\x9cI have stopped by your house a couple\ntimes and tried to call to make sure you have all the information you need\nto vote.\xe2\x80\x9d For a voter who wasn\xe2\x80\x99t home after two door visits and a phone\ncall, the next note would turn even more ominous: \xe2\x80\x9cI will keep checking the\nlist to see if you have voted and if not will drop by your house again to\nfollow up.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nOver the course of three weeks, Hazelwood\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cstalker test\xe2\x80\x9d deployed some of\nthe most potent psychological tricks known to nudge a citizen into the act\nof voting\xe2\x80\x94along with some new ones\xe2\x80\x94all as part of a borderline-creepy\ncourtship that would be difficult to ignore. After the election, in which\nRepublicans won the Senate races in all three of those states, Green and\nLenoir found that it had a considerable impact, increasing turnout among\ntargets by between two and three percentage points. It had not been easy,\nthough, as stalking demanded attention, resources, and planning that\ncampaigns cannot always muster for their field programs (The report did not\ncalculate the cost of such contact, and it is certainly conceivable that at\na much lower price yard signs could prove more cost-effective\xe2\x80\x94if less\ntargetable\xe2\x80\x94than the elaborate program that required the same field staffer\nto be available for every interaction with an assigned voter.)\n\n\n\nWhen Hazelwood presented the analysis to Priebus at the Capitol Hill Club,\nshe put the paper that Green and Lenoir had written into a thick, glossy\nbrochure with far more elaborate graphics than are typically packaged with\nacademic articles. Priebus says the findings confirm what he had assumed\nabout the value of localized field programs that focus on sustained\npersonal contact, but now \xe2\x80\x9cI can take that information and go sell it to\nthe state parties, and go to into a boardroom to get people to cut a\ncheck.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nThe finding that most excited both Hazelwood and Priebus, however, grew out\nof an accident. In Colorado, canvassers were mistakenly assigned to go to\nsome of the party\xe2\x80\x99s reliable voters, who had cast ballots in all four of\nthe most recent elections. After being hounded by a precinct captain, they\nvoted at a lower rate than those who received no contact at all. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have a\nproven fact now that we should stop calling these people,\xe2\x80\x9d Hazelwood says,\nwith comic impatience. \xe2\x80\x9cStop wasting time!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n*Republicans have won the battle over money in politics. Should anyone\ncelebrate?\n<http://www.washingtonpost.com/blogs/plum-line/wp/2015/07/07/republicans-have-won-the-battle-over-money-in-politics-should-anyone-celebrate/>\n// WaPo // Paul Waldman \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015 *\n\n\n\nAs the candidates for president have begun announcing their second-quarter\nfundraising hauls, a couple of things have become apparent. First, there is\na huge amount of money flowing into the campaign, even to some second-tier\ncandidates. And second, the campaign finance system is a joke.\n\n\n\nAs we now approach the anything-goes situation that Republicans have been\nadvocating for years, it\xe2\x80\x99s time to ask: Is this how we want things to work?\n\n\n\nRepublicans in particular should be asked that question, because they\xe2\x80\x99re\nthe ones who created this state of affairs and promised that it would be\ngood for everyone. Here are some of its characteristics: There will almost\ncertainly be more money spent in this election than ever before. A number\nof candidates have one billionaire benefactor promising to bankroll their\ncampaigns. The Federal Election Commission, deadlocked between its\nDemocratic and Republican members, has essentially stopped enforcing\ncampaign finance laws. The Internal Revenue Service has also all but given\nup enforcing the laws that govern tax-exempt nonprofits and their political\nactivities.\n\n\n\nThe campaigns are hauling in the cash. On Monday, Ted Cruz announced that\nhis campaign had $51 million dollars behind it, which included $37 million\nraised by pro-Cruz super PACs \xe2\x80\x94 so they\xe2\x80\x99re not even pretending that the\n\xe2\x80\x9cindependent\xe2\x80\x9d groups are anything but an arm of the Cruz campaign. Hillary\nClinton has raised $45 million just since April, and we\xe2\x80\x99re waiting to hear\nwhether the Jeb Bush super PAC Right to Rise met its $100 million goal.\n\n\n\nThis is all enabled by the fact that although campaigns and super PACs are\nprohibited from \xe2\x80\x9ccoordinating\xe2\x80\x9d with each other, what counts as coordination\nhas become so limited that it\xe2\x80\x99s little more than a speed bump for campaigns\nand super PACs that are working together. They have nothing to fear from\nthe FEC, where Republicans always appoint commissioners who barely believe\nthere ought to be any regulation of campaign finance at all, and have\nsuccessfully ground enforcement to a halt. The agency has gone two years\nwithout anyone leading its legal office, and its own chairwoman says: \xe2\x80\x9cThe\nlikelihood of the laws being enforced is slim.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nThe situation at the IRS, which has jurisdiction over nonprofit groups and\ntheir involvement in politics, is virtually the same. Because of the\n\xe2\x80\x9cscandal\xe2\x80\x9d that came to light in 2013, which involved extra scrutiny given\nto political groups applying for tax-exempt status as 501(c)(4) \xe2\x80\x9csocial\nwelfare\xe2\x80\x9d organizations, the agency came under withering attack from\nRepublicans and has shrunk back from enforcing the law.  Despite a new wave\nof secret donations going to phony social welfare organizations established\nby candidates to boost their campaigns, the IRS has essentially\nsurrendered, as the New York Times reports:\n\n\n\nAs presidential candidates find new ways to exploit secret donations from\ntax-exempt groups, hobbled regulators at the Internal Revenue Service\nappear certain to delay trying to curb widespread abuses at nonprofits\nuntil after the 2016 election.\n\n\n\nWe should take a moment to remember what the IRS scandal was actually\nabout. It wasn\xe2\x80\x99t, as so many conservatives believe, a story of a nefarious\nWhite House-directed conspiracy to persecute Barack Obama\xe2\x80\x99s enemies. In\nreality, there were a bunch of inadequately trained IRS employees\nattempting to apply a vaguely-worded law in the face of a dramatic increase\nin the number of applications they had to process after the 2010 Citizens\nUnited decision threw open the doors of campaign finance.\n\n\n\nAnd the groups that suffered the horrific injury of having their 501(c)(4)\napplications take longer to process than they should have? Let\xe2\x80\x99s not forget\nthat they were, in fact, violating the spirit of the law. Without question.\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s true of both the conservative and liberal groups who got extra\nscrutiny, which they absolutely deserved, because they were plainly\npolitical groups masquerading as social welfare organizations.\n\n\n\nBut now it\xe2\x80\x99s open season, and most of the presidential candidates have\nestablished 501(c)(4) organizations that are allowed to shield their\ndonors\xe2\x80\x99 identity from public scrutiny while operating as an arm of the\ncampaign. Marco Rubio\xe2\x80\x99s (c)(4), the Conservative Solutions Project, has\nalready raised $15.8 million for him.\n\n\n\nSo that\xe2\x80\x99s where we are \xe2\x80\x94 limitless donations from the wealthy and\ncorporations to super PACs and (c)(4)s that coordinate with campaigns,\nbillionaires buying more influence than ever, and crippled government\nagencies unable to enforce the frayed laws that still exist. To which\nRepublicans say, \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s great!\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nWhich isn\xe2\x80\x99t surprising, given that this was the system they engineered.\nThey filed lawsuit after lawsuit in a (largely successful) attempt to get\nthe Supreme Court to gut the campaign finance laws that had been in place\nfor decades, opening up the system to more money \xe2\x80\x94 while they also files\nsuits challenging every attempt at public financing in any form that might\nlevel the playing field. They threw sand in the FEC\xe2\x80\x99s gears and bludgeoned\nthe IRS into submission. And they did it all on the assumption that the\nmore money that pours into campaigns and the less accountability there is,\nthe more partisan advantage they\xe2\x80\x99ll gain.\n\n\n\nWhich might or might not be true \xe2\x80\x94 after all, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t as though Democratic\npoliticians like Obama and Clinton have had too much trouble exploiting the\nsystem and raising spectacular amounts of money themselves. But we\xe2\x80\x99ve now\njust about reached the campaign finance nirvana Republicans had been\nworking toward for so long.\n\n\n\nSo how\xe2\x80\x99s it working out? Is there anyone who could argue that this is the\nbest of all possible systems? Is there someone who\xe2\x80\x99ll say that the American\npublic benefits when billionaires summon presidential candidates to grovel\nbefore them in attempt to secure their patronage? Is there a single person\nwho\xe2\x80\x99ll honestly say, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, this is exactly how campaigns ought to work\xe2\x80\x9d?\nCan anybody make the case that this will produce a more democratic system\nthat helps the government solve problems and secure progress for the public?\n\n\n\n*OTHER 2016 NEWS*\n\n\n\n*\xe2\x80\x98Super PACs\xe2\x80\x99 Take On New Role, Organizing Voters\n<http://www.nytimes.com/2015/07/08/us/politics/super-pacs-take-on-new-role-organizing-voters.html?_r=0>\n// NYT // Trip Gabriel \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015 *\n\n\n\nCollege students supporting Rand Paul for president were out in force going\ndoor to door in this university town recently, using their iPad Minis to\nhelp identify Republicans committed to voting for the Kentucky senator in\nnext year\xe2\x80\x99s Iowa caucuses.\n\n\n\nAnd when Gov. Bobby Jindal of Louisiana visited the state the same week in\nJune for town hall-style meetings in Waukee, Council Bluffs and Sioux City,\nhis supporters collected voters\xe2\x80\x99 names and emails with an eye toward caucus\nday.\n\n\n\nBut the Paul and Jindal campaigns were not behind these time-honored\ngrass-roots organizing efforts. Instead, in a twist that shows how even\nretail politics is being transformed by a flood of loosely regulated big\nmoney, the two candidates\xe2\x80\x99 courtship of voters is being carried out by\n\xe2\x80\x9csuper PACs,\xe2\x80\x9d which are using their abundant war chests to move into the\nnuts and bolts of campaign operations.\n\n\n\nIn previous election cycles, super PACs \xe2\x80\x94 which can raise unlimited\ndonations from corporations and individuals alike \xe2\x80\x94 largely channeled money\nfrom wealthy donors into political advertising. But now they are branching\nout into what had seemed a fundamental function of a campaign committee:\norganizing voters one at a time.\n\n\n\nThe 2016 race could be the most expensive in history, in part because of\nspending by \xe2\x80\x9csuper PACs.\xe2\x80\x9d Legally, the groups cannot coordinate with\ncandidates, but there is plenty of wiggle room. By Aaron Byrd and Emily B.\nHager on Publish Date July 7, 2015.\n\nCandidates \xe2\x80\x9care pushing as much of the stuff as they can over to the super\nPACs because that\xe2\x80\x99s where the real money is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Terry Giles, who\nrecently quit as campaign manager for Ben Carson, the retired neurosurgeon,\nto start a new super PAC that will support him.\n\n\n\nThe practice of having super PACS, which can be entirely financed by a\nsingle billionaire, take over such operations allows campaigns, which may\nraise only $2,700 from any one donor, to outsource the costly,\nlabor-intensive work of recruiting activists and building lists of\nsupporters.\n\n\n\nIt could also allow second-tier candidates to be more competitive,\nprolonging the nominating process. And in the general election, a\nRepublican nominee whose organizing is paid for by a super PAC might level\nthe playing field with Democrats, whose allies in labor have traditionally\ngiven the party an edge in mustering volunteers to help turn out voters.\n\n\n\nBut there are risks to outsourcing a field campaign. Candidates, who are\nlegally forbidden to coordinate with super PACs, are in danger of being cut\noff from their most ardent supporters as they head into caucus and primary\nelections.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs a campaign, you have absolutely no idea\xe2\x80\x9d how you\xe2\x80\x99re doing, said Nick\nRyan, president of a super PAC supporting Mike Huckabee, the former\nArkansas governor. \xe2\x80\x9cThat would scare me to death. You\xe2\x80\x99d be disconnected\nfrom the very base of your campaign, the people who are most passionate\nabout you.\xe2\x80\x9d (Still, Mr. Ryan hesitated to rule out any grass-roots campaign\nwork aside from recruiting volunteers to support Mr. Huckabee.)\n\n\n\nTina Goff, who recruited leaders in all 99 Iowa counties on behalf of a\nsuper PAC supporting Ben Carson before he even declared his candidacy,\nturned down an offer of a job with his campaign. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would have been great\nto travel around with Dr. Carson,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I felt like I had created\nan infrastructure here and I wanted to continue what I was doing.\xe2\x80\x9d She said\nshe was now hiring 25 people to knock on doors in Iowa after paying $40,000\nfor a list of Republican caucusgoers from the state party.\n\n\n\nEven for candidates who do not have fund-raising problems, relying on super\nPACs offers another way to manage expectations and perceptions.\n\n\n\nIn Iowa, where Jeb Bush faces stiff resistance from conservatives, a senior\nRepublican strategist said that before Mr. Bush announced his candidacy,\nhis team discussed leaving some of the field organizing to a super PAC\nestablished by his aides. That would spare Mr. Bush\xe2\x80\x99s campaign some\nembarrassment should he perform badly in Iowa during the caucuses by giving\nhis campaign distance from the effort.\n\n\n\nSeveral campaigns have welcomed outside organizing efforts in Iowa, which\nwill hold the first nominating contest on Feb. 1. The farthest along are\nefforts for Mr. Paul, Carly Fiorina and Mr. Carson.\n\n\n\nAn outside group that supports Gov. Scott Walker of Wisconsin, who is\nexpected to announce his candidacy next week, collected cards at a recent\npolitical forum in Des Moines from voters pledging to caucus for the\ncandidate. A spokesman for the group, Our American Revival, said it would\ncontinue to organize on Mr. Walker\xe2\x80\x99s behalf in Iowa and other states.\n\n\n\nSo far, only super PACs supporting Republicans appear to be organizing the\ngrass roots, although outside groups that back Democrats, including labor\nunions and nonprofits, have long performed tasks like registering voters\nand canvassing neighborhoods. (A super PAC aimed at generating grass-roots\nsupport for Hillary Rodham Clinton, Ready for Hillary, closed up shop once\nMrs. Clinton formally entered the race.)\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s no surprise that super PACs would at some point evolve into the\norganizational side of politics,\xe2\x80\x9d said Chris LaCivita, a senior adviser to\nMr. Paul. He called it a predictable outgrowth of an era that since 2002\nhas banned unlimited donations to party committees, and in which wealthy\ndonors have rechanneled support to outside groups, which are less\naccountable.\n\n\n\nMs. Fiorina, the former chief executive of Hewlett-Packard, may have turned\nover more traditional campaign tasks to a super PAC than any other\ncandidate: lining up endorsements, answering reporters\xe2\x80\x99 questions about her\nbusiness career and opening field offices in early primary states like\nSouth Carolina. The primary reason is her difficulty raising money for a\nfull-fledged campaign. She reported pulling in just $1.4 million in May and\nJune.\n\n\n\nThe super PAC, Carly for America, which said Monday that it had raised $3.4\nmillion, \xe2\x80\x9cis running a grass-roots organization focused on building support\nin early states,\xe2\x80\x9d said Katie Hughes, a spokeswoman.\n\n\n\nThe group organizing for Mr. Paul, Concerned American Voters, has pledges\nof \xe2\x80\x9cmultiple millions\xe2\x80\x9d from wealthy donors in Silicon Valley, said Jeff\nFrazee, its president. It has had 40 full-time field staffers in Iowa since\nJune 1, knocking on doors during the day and making phone calls at night.\n\n\n\nBut that effort illustrates the potential for duplication and clashing\nagendas between super PACs and campaigns. Mr. Paul already had a network of\nactivists in Iowa, many of whom supported campaigns by his father, Ron\nPaul. Asked about the super PAC, the campaign\xe2\x80\x99s state director, Steve\nGrubbs, said in a text message: \xe2\x80\x9cNot commenting on this. Wish I could.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nThat was because the donors were talking among themselves about shifting\nsupport from TV advertising, the focus of America\xe2\x80\x99s Liberty PAC, to a\ngrass-roots campaign.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not convinced that TV ads are really as effective as they were,\xe2\x80\x9d said\nMr. Banister, an early investor in PayPal, adding that interactive\ncommunication with voters mattered more. \xe2\x80\x9cGrass roots is just kind of\nold-school interactive,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\n\n\n\nMr. Frazee, the Paul backer, said he was inspired by Barack Obama\xe2\x80\x99s upset\nvictory over Mrs. Clinton in Iowa eight years ago. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was able to win with\na very organized ground game,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s been our strategy.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nIn Iowa City, Ani DeGroot, a college student on summer break working for\nConcerned American Voters, approached Timothy Anderson, who was mowing his\nlawn.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you support Rand Paul?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked after he had shut down the mower.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI probably will,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cFantastic,\xe2\x80\x9d Ms. DeGroot said. \xe2\x80\x9cAre there any issues that are most\nimportant to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish we would just get out of all these wars and being policemen to the\nworld,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Anderson, a human resources manager.\n\n\n\nMs. DeGroot entered his responses into her iPad Mini supplied by the super\nPAC. At a future date, another Paul supporter might call on Mr. Anderson to\nurge him to volunteer, or even to speak for Mr. Paul at his precinct\ncaucus. The vote may be less than seven months away, but it is never too\nearly to begin organizing.\n\n\n\n*Puerto Rican debt crisis forces its way onto presidential political agenda\n<http://www.washingtonpost.com/business/economy/puerto-rican-debt-crisis-forces-its-way-onto-presidential-political-agenda/2015/07/07/40ea8aae-1ffe-11e5-aeb9-a411a84c9d55_story.html>\n// WaPo // Steven Mufson - July 8, 2015*\n\n\n\nQ: Which place has as many Republican delegates as New Hampshire, plus\nenough native sons and daughters living in Florida to swing the outcome of\nthat state\xe2\x80\x99s presidential primaries or even the general election?\n\n\n\nA: Puerto Rico.\n\n\n\nThat political reality has put the financially ailing U.S. territory on the\nmap of presidential hopefuls, who are being asked what, if anything, the\nfederal government should do to help engineer a rescue plan for the\ndebt-strapped island.\n\n\n\nPolitically, much is at stake. Puerto Rico has its own voice at nominating\nconventions, with more delegates at the Democratic Convention than 21\nstates. And 1 million Puerto Ricans live in Florida, enough to be the swing\nvote in one of the nation\xe2\x80\x99s most important swing states.\n\n\n\nAt the current pace of migration, triple the rate just a few years ago, the\nnumber of people of Puerto Rican origin in Florida could soon exceed the\nnumber of Cuban Americans in the state. Along Interstate 4 between Orlando\nand Tampa in central Florida, Puerto Rican Americans are everywhere,\ncomprising more than a quarter of the population of Florida\xe2\x80\x99s Osceola\nCounty.\n\n\n\nAs a result, presidential candidates are being pressed to voice support for\nPuerto Rico in its hour of economic need and back the use of bankruptcy law\nto help the territory, which is teetering on the edge of default, to reduce\nand manage its $73 billion in debt. While the bankruptcy code can be used\nby states for the orderly court-supervised reorganization of cities,\nmunicipalities, municipal utilities or school districts, Congress in 1984\naltered the law to bar the commonwealth of Puerto Rico or any of its\nutilities, towns or school districts from being able to turn to the\nbankruptcy code in a crisis.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose who want the support of Puerto Ricans must help Puerto Rico now. Not\nlater.\xe2\x80\x9d Puerto Rico Gov. Alejandro Garc\xc3\xada Padilla said in an interview\nSunday on Telemundo. He added, \xe2\x80\x9cThey need to act now, not in a few months\nduring the elections.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nGarc\xc3\xada Padilla said \xe2\x80\x9cthere are some people who have been a little passive,\nand they are the candidates for president of the United States. Both the\nDemocrats and the Republicans, especially in Florida, because as you know,\nit\xe2\x80\x99s a battleground state and it\xe2\x80\x99s important.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nAnd the Puerto Rican vote is up for grabs, both in Florida and the\nterritory. Six Americans of Puerto Rican descent have been elected to the\nFlorida state legislature \xe2\x80\x94 three Republicans and three Democrats. In\nPuerto Rico, the current governor, Garc\xc3\xada Padilla, who narrowly won\nelection, calls himself a friend of President Obama\xe2\x80\x99s. The previous\ngovernor, Luis G. Fortu\xc3\xb1o, resembled a Reagan Republican.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDuring the last campaign, it was called the swing vote of the swing\nstate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Jeffrey Farrow, chairman of the Oliver Group, a public policy\ncommunications firm, and a former White House adviser on Puerto Rico under\nPresidents Jimmy Carter and Bill Clinton.\n\n\n\nA poll by Latino Decisions, a firm that does polling and analysis of Latino\nvoters, showed that Puerto Rican voters in Florida went 72 percent to 28\npercent for Obama over Mitt Romney in 2012, while Cuban American voters in\nthe state chose Romney over Obama by 64 percent to 35 percent.\n\n\n\nYet Farrow said that while \xe2\x80\x9cthere is a perception that Puerto Ricans are\nDemocratic voters, that is not necessarily the case in Florida. The more\nrecent poll data shows they identify more with Democrats than Republicans,\nbut they are socially conservative. They voted for Jeb Bush for governor.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cDuring my race, I said I would slash government expenses,\xe2\x80\x9d said Fortu\xc3\xb1o,\nnow a partner at Steptoe & Johnson, a Washington-based law firm. \xe2\x80\x9cOften on\nradio talk shows, people would say they have a daughter who needs a job,\nand I\xe2\x80\x99d say she would not have a job in government, and my advisers would\ngo nuts. And I won. So it\xe2\x80\x99s very doable.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nJeb Bush has been courting the Puerto Rican vote for a long time. In 1980,\nhe spent more than two months organizing the campaign of his father, George\nH.W. Bush, who handily won the primary that year. Jeb Bush delivered\nspeeches in Spanish at more than 75 gatherings. When he campaigned to be\ngovernor of Florida, he went to Puerto Rican church fiestas.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think statehood is the best path, personally. I\xe2\x80\x99ve believed that for a\nlong, long while,\xe2\x80\x9d Jeb Bush said during a visit to the territory in April,\nrecalling his father\xe2\x80\x99s long-ago campaign. \xe2\x80\x9cNothing makes me believe that\nthat position should change. To get the full benefits and responsibilities\nof citizenship, being a state is the only path to make that happen.\xe2\x80\x9d He\nsaid: \xe2\x80\x9cI think the next president, whoever that person is, should use their\ninfluence to make sure Congress acts on this and that this should be a\nquestion of self-determination. . . . That\xe2\x80\x99s just a question of principle\nand, you know, morality, I think. It\xe2\x80\x99s not a question of politics.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nBush, while campaigning in Puerto Rico in April and again in South Carolina\nlast week, also came out in support of granting Puerto Rico the option of\nusing Chapter 9 of the bankruptcy code to restructure its debts. \xe2\x80\x9cPuerto\nRico should be given the same rights as the states,\xe2\x80\x9d Bush said in Spanish\nbefore a small crowd at the Universidad Metropolitana in San Juan.\n\n\n\nBush\xe2\x80\x99s Republican rivals have been, by and large, more cautious for fear\nthat extending Chapter 9 to Puerto Rico could look like a bailout of the\nisland \xe2\x80\x94 even though bankruptcy extracts concessions from bondholders and\nrequires no federal money.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSenator [Marco] Rubio has been closely watching events in Puerto Rico and\nis concerned about the economic situation there,\xe2\x80\x9d spokeswoman Brooke Sammon\ntold The Washington Post by e-mail over the weekend. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s in the process\nof reviewing the legislation to make sure it is the right approach to begin\naddressing Puerto Rico\xe2\x80\x99s debt crisis without having any negative impact on\nAmerican taxpayers.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nDonald Trump \xe2\x80\x94 whose heavily indebted corporate affiliates have used\nbankruptcy to reorganize four times \xe2\x80\x94 has a unique perspective on Puerto\nRico. He is also a partner with local construction tycoons Arturo and Jorge\nL. Diaz in the Trump International Golf Club, a resort east of San Juan\nthat boasts two 18-hole courses. The Trump campaign did not respond to a\nrequest for comment.\n\n\n\nIn a June 30 tweet, Democratic presidential front-runner Hillary Rodham\nClinton did not address the bankruptcy issue but said that \xe2\x80\x9cPuerto Rico\xe2\x80\x99s\ndebt crisis is not theirs alone. For PR\xe2\x80\x99s economy to grow & their people to\nthrive, they need real tools & real support.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nBut on Tuesday, Clinton said that Puerto Rican municipalities and\nstate-owned enterprises should be allowed to use bankruptcy protections to\noverhaul their finances. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re not talking about a bailout, we\xe2\x80\x99re talking\nabout a fair shot at success,\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a statement.\n\n\n\nFormer Maryland governor Martin O\xe2\x80\x99Malley (D), by contrast, jumped on the\nissue last week. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very concerned about the impending financial\ncollapse of Puerto Rico,\xe2\x80\x9d said O\xe2\x80\x99Malley, who backed the use of the\nbankruptcy code and an equalization of Medicaid and Medicare benefits for\nthe island\xe2\x80\x99s residents. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must help our fellow U.S. citizens, not only\nbecause it\xe2\x80\x99s the right thing to do, but because our region\xe2\x80\x99s economic\nstability depends on it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\n\n\n\nSen. Bernie Sanders (I-Vt.) joined the discussion on Tuesday, backing\nbankruptcy protections to enable Puerto Rico to \xe2\x80\x9crestructure its debt in a\nrational way that does not harm its people, ordinary investors or pension\nfunds in the United States.\xe2\x80\x9d He blamed the island\xe2\x80\x99s debt crisis on \xe2\x80\x9cthe\npolicies of austerity and the greed of large financial institutions.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nThe flow of Puerto Ricans to Florida has altered the calculus of politics\nthere. Whereas politicians once catered to the conservative, anti-Castro\nCuban opinion that dominated the Latino vote, now the Cuban American\nconsensus on Cuba has fractured and the Puerto Rican population has swelled.\n\n\n\nThat Puerto Rican electorate has its own concerns.\n\n\n\nAccording to polling data gathered last November by Latino Decisions,\nPuerto Ricans support expanding Medicaid and raising the minimum wage more\nstrongly than Cuban Americans do, although Cuban Americans also strongly\nendorse those policy proposals. Education also ranks higher as a concern\namong Puerto Ricans.\n\n\n\nYet despite Jeb Bush\xe2\x80\x99s efforts, the Latino Decisions poll in November\nshowed that among Puerto Ricans in Florida, he was then getting a 42\npercent \xe2\x80\x9cvery unfavorable\xe2\x80\x9d rating and only 10 percent \xe2\x80\x9cvery favorable.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nNow the territory\xe2\x80\x99s economic plight might become a factor. And people in\nFlorida keep track through readily available media from the island as well\nas locally.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Puerto Ricans living here are very, very strongly connected to the\nisland,\xe2\x80\x9d said Betsy Franceschini, who is Florida regional director of the\nPuerto Rico Federal Affairs Administration, an arm of the Puerto Rican\ngovernment. \xe2\x80\x9cThey do care even though they\xe2\x80\x99re here.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nFranceschini operates her agency in a modest house in Kissimmee with two\nflagpoles, one for the Puerto Rican flag and one for the American. The\nagency provides advice to Puerto Ricans moving from the island to the\nOrlando-Tampa area. The previous governor, Fortu\xc3\xb1o, had closed the office.\nGarc\xc3\xada Padilla, fulfilling a campaign promise he made during a visit to\nOrlando, reopened it.\n\n\n\nFranceschini said the key public-policy issues of concern to people who\ncome to the office for help are the island\xe2\x80\x99s fiscal crisis, its desire to\nuse the bankruptcy code to restructure municipal debts and unequal Medicare\npayments by the federal government. \xe2\x80\x9cFolks here are very upset about that\nand are organizing and mobilizing,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cand they want to know where\nthe candidates stand.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n*Christie, Paul and Perry Court the Black Vote\n<http://www.wsj.com/articles/christie-paul-and-perry-court-the-black-vote-1436305667>\n// WSJ // Jason Riley \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015 *\n\n\n\nPresident Obama will be leaving the White House in 18 months, but where\nwill that leave the minority voters who helped elect him? It\xe2\x80\x99s a question\nthat Republican presidential hopefuls not named Donald Trump are pondering.\n\n\n\nBetween 1980 and 2004, black support for the Democratic presidential\ncandidates ranged from 83% to 90%. But in 2008 Mr. Obama won 95% of the\nblack vote and was re-elected four years later with 93%. In 2012 black\nvoters turned out at higher rates than white voters for the first time on\nrecord. The likelihood of Hillary Clinton or another Democratic nominee\ndrawing Obama levels of black support is small. Many blacks may simply stay\nhome. Others, having observed the black economic retrogression that\noccurred even with a black Democrat in the Oval Office, might consider\nalternatives.\n\n\n\nTo that end, Republicans such as New Jersey Gov. Chris Christie and\nKentucky Sen. Rand Paul have been actively courting minorities. Mr.\nChristie has become a familiar presence in Camden, Newark and other\neconomically depressed parts of the Garden State. In 2013 he was re-elected\nwith the backing of 51% of Hispanics and 21% of blacks\xe2\x80\x94an impressive\n19-point and 12-point increase, respectively, from four years earlier.\n\n\n\nWoody Allen once said that 80% of success is showing up, and Mr. Christie\nhas been willing to campaign in communities and among voters that\nRepublicans too often write off. The governor has bothered to introduce\nhimself to black voters directly instead of letting Democratic opponents\ndefine him or only giving the occasional speech to the NAACP.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many Republicans are you going to see walking up and down these\nstreets like this?\xe2\x80\x9d a black woman in Orange, N.J., told a Real Clear\nPolitics reporter in 2013. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s approachable. I really like that. And I\nthink the people in Orange are really thrilled, whether they\xe2\x80\x99re Democrat or\nRepublican, that he\xe2\x80\x99s here.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nMr. Paul, for his part, has courted younger blacks by visiting historically\nblack college campuses, which is admirable. Less admirable is Mr. Paul\xe2\x80\x99s\ndecision to mouth liberal positions on voter ID, drug laws and prison\nsentencing in order to win black support. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m trying to go out and say to\nAfrican-Americans \xe2\x80\x98I want your vote, and the Republican Party wants your\nvote,\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9d he told a radio interviewer last year, explaining why he thinks\nthe GOP should drop the voter ID issue. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have to be aware that the\nperception is out there and be careful about not so overdoing something\nthat we further alienate a block of people that we need to attract.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nThat\xe2\x80\x99s not black outreach\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s pandering. Moreover, it\xe2\x80\x99s off-base. A Fox\nNews poll last year found majority support for voter ID laws among every\ndemographic group, including 51% of blacks. A 2012 Pew survey put black\nsupport for voter ID at 62%. And law-abiding ghetto residents want the\ncriminals who prey on them and their children locked up, not coddled. Don\xe2\x80\x99t\nconfuse the agenda of civil-rights groups and other black elites with the\ndesires of most blacks.\n\n\n\nThe better approach is the one taken by former Texas Gov. Rick Perry, who\nlaunched his second bid for the White House last month and gave one of the\ncampaign\xe2\x80\x99s most poignant speeches last week. Mr. Perry is less concerned\nwith making race-based entreaties than he is with appealing to blacks as\nfellow Americans. Imagine that!\n\n\n\nThe governor acknowledged America\xe2\x80\x99s history of oppressing blacks\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cand\nbecause slavery and segregation were sanctioned by government, there is a\nrole for government policy in addressing their lasting effects\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94but he also\nnoted the tremendous progress that has been made.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cA half-century ago, Republicans and Democrats came together to finally\nenshrine in the law the principle that all of us\xe2\x80\x94regardless of race, color\nor national origin\xe2\x80\x94are created equal,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Perry. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen it comes to\nrace, America is a better and more tolerant and more welcoming place than\nit has ever been.\xe2\x80\x9d These may be plain truths, but such progress is\nregularly dismissed by many on the grievance-focused left, where there is\nmoney to be made by civil-rights groups, and votes to be had by Democratic\npoliticians, who are willing to pretend that systemic racism still drives\nracial disparities in the U.S.\n\n\n\nMr. Perry believes that a more significant barrier to black progress is\npoor political representation. The Democratic Party has long operated with\nno fear of losing the black vote, and Republicans have been too quick to\nconcede it. Mr. Perry\xe2\x80\x99s overarching theme, however, was a rebuke of\nidentity politics, stressing instead what all Americans desire\xe2\x80\x94safe\nneighborhoods, good schools, economic opportunity. And he urged black\nvoters to compare the track records of liberal and conservative governance\non those fronts and then hold politicians accountable.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the cities where the left-wing solutions have been tried over and over\nagain\xe2\x80\x94places like Detroit and Chicago and Baltimore\xe2\x80\x94African-Americans are\nmoving out,\xe2\x80\x9d said the governor. \xe2\x80\x9cIn blue-state coastal cities, you have\nstrict zoning laws and environmental regulations that have prevented\nbuilders from expanding the housing supply.\xe2\x80\x9d San Francisco\xe2\x80\x99s black\npopulation today is half of what it was in 1970. That\xe2\x80\x99s obviously not\nbecause conservatives run the place.\n\n\n\nIf the GOP is getting serious about competing for black voters, it\xe2\x80\x99s a\nwelcome development for our political system. And if Republican\npresidential candidates are looking for the right approach, they could do a\nlot worse than Mr. Perry\xe2\x80\x99s template.\n\n\n\n*2016ers tiptoe around Puerto Rico\'s debt bomb\n<http://www.politico.com/story/2015/07/2016ers-tiptoe-around-puerto-ricos-debt-bomb-119820.html>\n// Politico // Daniel Strauss \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015 *\n\n\n\nWith Puerto Rico asking the U.S. federal government for a lifeline to\nrescue it from crushing debt, some 2016 candidates see an opportunity \xe2\x80\x93\nFlorida votes.\n\n\n\nJeb Bush was first out of the gates. Back in April, as the island territory\nstarted careening toward a breaking point, he said Puerto Rico\xe2\x80\x99s public\nagencies should be able to file for bankruptcy.\n\n\n\nBut late last month, Puerto Rico Gov. Alejandro Garc\xc3\xada Padilla announced\nthat the commonwealth could not pay back its roughly $72 billion in debts,\nlending the question new urgency.\n\n\n\nDemocrats have urged Washington to help. On Tuesday, frontrunner Hillary\nClinton called on Congress to allow Puerto Rico to restructure its debts\nunder Chapter 9 of the Bankruptcy Code, the first step in what she called a\n\xe2\x80\x9cmultifaceted\xe2\x80\x9d approach. Her top rival for the Democratic nomination,\nVermont Sen. Bernie Sanders, followed suit a few hours later. Former\nMaryland governor Martin O\xe2\x80\x99Malley had previously pressed President Barack\nObama and Congress \xe2\x80\x9cto act to ensure that Puerto Rico is able to negotiate\nwith its creditors.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nBut the rest of the 2016 field, including the other four senators running\nfor president, has been conspicuously slower to take a position. Sens. Ted\nCruz, Lindsey Graham, Rand Paul did not respond to questions on the issue.\nSen. Marco Rubio\xe2\x80\x99s office said the Florida senator was still looking into\nit.\n\n\n\nNone were willing to throw their support behind a bill being offered up by\nSens. Charles Schumer and Richard Blumenthal that would give Puerto Rico\nthe same bankruptcy protection as municipalities across the rest of the\nUnited States.\n\n\n\nPuerto Rico\xe2\x80\x99s debt crisis has the potential to affect the 2016 race \xe2\x80\x94 and\nnowhere more so than in Florida, with its growing Puerto Rican population\nof about 1 million people, a key constituency in a purple state with 29\nelectoral votes.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a driver for their vote,\xe2\x80\x9d said Resident Commissioner Pedro\nPierluisi, Puerto Rico\xe2\x80\x99s representative on Capitol Hill. \xe2\x80\x9cOur votes are\nswing votes.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nIn the last two presidential elections, Puerto Rican votes boosted Obama in\nFlorida, but could easily help Republicans this time around, former Puerto\nRican governor Luis Fortuno said.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey can sway,\xe2\x80\x9d Fortuno, a conservative, said. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe other\nRepublicans that want to compete effectively ought to be thinking about\nthis, and they probably have to say something about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see how you get through the Florida primary without being asked,\xe2\x80\x9d\nsaid Douglas Holtz-Eakin, a former top economic adviser for Sen. John\nMcCain\xe2\x80\x99s 2008 presidential bid.\n\n\n\nBut calling for federal intervention is not without political risks: No\nRepublican presidential candidate wants to be tarred as pro-bailout.\n\n\n\nWhile bankruptcy protection would not provide the island territory with\ndirect financial support, it could still raise the hackles of creditors\neager to toss around the \xe2\x80\x9cb\xe2\x80\x9d word in a bid to sap congressional momentum.\n\n\n\nThe issue is especially perilous for Rubio, who rode to office in 2010 on a\nwave of support from tea party voters vehemently opposed to government\nrescues of big banks and underwater homeowners.\n\n\n\nSo even though Rubio is locked with Bush in a zero-sum struggle with Bush\nfor Florida primary votes, especially among Hispanics, he has been hesitant\nto weigh in.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cSenator Rubio has been closely watching events in Puerto Rico and is\nconcerned about the economic situation there,\xe2\x80\x9d Rubio spokeswoman Brooke\nSammon told POLITICO. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s in the process of reviewing the legislation to\nmake sure it is the right approach to begin addressing Puerto Rico\xe2\x80\x99s debt\ncrisis without having any negative impact on American taxpayers.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\nPierluisi said it\xe2\x80\x99s understandable why Rubio and Ted Cruz, the other 2016\ncandidate of Cuban descent, have been so cautious.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know for a fact that both of them have been studying this issue and\ntheir staff have been getting their feet wet on this,\xe2\x80\x9d Pierluisi said.\n\n\n\nFormer Arkansas governor Mike Huckabee has already previewed how the more\nconservative candidates might approach the issue.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve had enough bailouts. Puerto Rico is a commonwealth, not a state, and\nshould not be allowed to file for Chapter 9 bankruptcy,\xe2\x80\x9d Huckabee told\nPOLITICO.\n\n\n\nEven among the candidates who support allowing Puerto Rico to file for\nbankruptcy, there was a visible cautiousness about using the dreaded term.\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re not talking about a bailout, we\xe2\x80\x99re talking about a fair shot at\nsuccess,\xe2\x80\x9d Clinton said in Tuesday\xe2\x80\x99s statement.\n\n\n\n*Jeb Bush, Hillary Clinton to share stage in Fort Lauderdale\n<http://www.tampabay.com/blogs/the-buzz-florida-politics/jeb-bush-hillary-clinton-to-share-stage-in-fort-lauderdale/2236438>\n// Tampa Bay Times // Alex Leary \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015 *\n\n\n\nJeb Bush will speak before the National Urban League Conference in Fort\nLauderdale on July 31, illustrating his campaign\'s goal of broadening the\nGOP message.\n\n\n\nHillary Clinton is also addressing the group, as is Ben Carson and Martin\nO\'Malley.\n\n\n\n"The candidates will share their visions for saving our cities on Friday,\nJuly 31, during a session entitled \'Off To The Races: The 2016 Presidential\nCandidates\xe2\x80\x99 Plenary,\' " according to a release.\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs we convene in Florida to deliberate solutions to the economic and\nsocial challenges our cities are facing, it\xe2\x80\x99s vital that those contending\nfor the highest office in the land be part of that conversation,\xe2\x80\x9d National\nUrban League President and CEO Marc H. Morial said.\n\n\n\nThe candidates\xe2\x80\x99 plenary will take place on the second full day of the\nConference themed \xe2\x80\x9cSave Our Cities: Education, Jobs + Justice.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\n\n\n\xe2\x80\x9cOur focus was inspired by a year that saw little accountability for law\nenforcement responsible for killing unarmed Black men, teenagers and\nchildren; a continual assault on voting rights; widening economic\ninequality gaps; and an increasingly partisan education debate far more\nrooted in political agendas than in putting our children first,\xe2\x80\x9d Morial\nsaid.\n\n\n\n*Jeb Bush and Hillary Clinton support bankruptcy rights for Puerto Rico\n<http://money.cnn.com/2015/07/07/investing/puerto-rico-chapter-9-bankruptcy/>\n// CNN \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015 *\n\n\n\nPuerto Rico has $73 billion in debt that it says it can\'t repay. Now it has\ntwo very powerful allies on its side: Jeb Bush and Hillary Clinton.\n\n\n\nBoth 2016 presidential candidates support giving Puerto Rico the same legal\nbankruptcy rights that states have. Puerto Rico\'s governor has been\nlobbying hard for that right.\n\nThere\'s a bill in Congress to give Puerto Rico what it wants, but it has no\nco-sponsors. That\'s why Bush and Clinton\'s backing could be key.\n\n\n\n"I think that Puerto Rico ought to be treated as other states are treated\nas it relates to restructuring," Bush said during a visit to the island in\nlate April. Other Republican candidates have largely stayed silent on the\nissue thus far.\n\n\n\nOn Tuesday, Clinton jumped on board as well.\n\n\n\n"Congress should provide Puerto Rico the same authority that states already\nhave to enable severely distressed government entities, including\nmunicipalities and public corporations, to restructure their debts," she\nsaid in a statement on her campaign website.\n\n\n\nFormer Maryland governor Martin O\'Malley has been a vocal supporter as well.\n\n\n\nWhat it means: To be clear, no state can file for bankruptcy. But the 50\nstates do have the right to allow municipalities -- cities, towns and other\nlocal government entities -- within their states to file for bankruptcy.\n\n\n\nPuerto Rican municipalities want to be able to file for what is known as\nChapter 9 bankruptcy like Detroit and Vallejo, California did.\n\n\n\nThe benefit of bankruptcy is that is allows a broke government to shed some\nof its debt -- if a judge approve a municipality\'s game plan.\n\n\n\nWill Congress act? The bill in Congress would give Puerto Rico the same\nrights that states have under Chapter 9. So far, the bill\'s only supporter\nin Congress is Puerto Rico\'s representative, who get to vote.\n\n\n\nIt might seem like a small issue in Washington, but it\'s a big issue for\nfinancial markets.\n\nPuerto Rico has a tiny population -- about the size of Connecticut -- but\nit has a debt the size of a big state like New York. Over 20% of U.S. bond\nfunds hold Puerto Rican debt.\n\nThe island doesn\'t have enough money to pay back its creditors. Some have\neven taken to calling it "America\'s Greece." Governor Alejandro Garc\xc3\xada\nPadilla says Puerto Rico is in a "death spiral."\n\n\n\nA lot of the trouble stems from Puerto Rico\'s power company, known as\nPREPA. It alone has $9 billion. It would be a game changer if PREPA could\ndeclare bankruptcy.\n\n\n\nWhy bankruptcy? "Chapter 9 is really the only way to impose some sort of\nrestructuring on all the bondholders," says David Tawil, a lawyer and\npresident of Maglan Capital.\n\nBond holders don\'t want Puerto Rico to get that because it opens the\npossibility that they would be forced to take a loss on their bond\ninvestment if a bankruptcy judge sides with the island.\n\n\n\nBut the proposal has little currency in Washington.\n\n\n\n"The bill that is in Congress has no co-sponsors and no support in\nCongress, although it does have a fair amount of support among bankruptcy\nexperts," says Juliet Moringiello, a professor at Widener University School\nof Law and a former scholar of the American Bankruptcy Institute.\n\n\n\nMoringiello notes that Chapter 9 bankruptcy is rare, although the majority\nof filings have been public hospitals and utilities like PREPA, not cities.\n\n\n\nWhere the candidates stand: Jeb Bush has been a vocal supporter of\nstatehood for Puerto Rico -- if the island\'s citizens want that.\n\n\n\n"I think statehood is the best path, personally," Bush said in April. Since\nthen, he has clarified that he thinks the island needs to make a number of\nchanges to deal with its debt beyond just declaring bankruptcy, including\nreducing some benefits for workers.\n\nOn the Democratic side, O\'Malley was an early supporter of Puerto Rico\ngetting Chapter 9 bankruptcy rights. Senator Bernie Sanders has voiced his\nsupport for Greece, but has yet to say much on Puerto Rico.\n\n\n\n*OPINIONS/EDITORIALS/BLOGS*\n\n\n\n*The non-Clinton alternative for Democrats\n<http://www.washingtonpost.com/opinions/the-non-clinton-alternative-for-democrats/2015/07/06/9b8d33ec-241a-11e5-b72c-2b7d516e1e0e_story.html>\n// WaPo // Eugene Robinson \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015 *\n\n\n\nIs Bernie Sanders the political reincarnation of Eugene McCarthy? I doubt\nit, but let\xe2\x80\x99s hope he makes the Democratic presidential race interesting.\n\n\n\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know if front-runner Hillary Clinton shares my wish, but she ought\nto. I\xe2\x80\x99m not of the school that believes competition for competition\xe2\x80\x99s sake\nis always a good thing. But Sanders has an appeal for younger, more\nliberal, more idealistic Democrats that Clinton presently lacks. If she\ncompetes for these voters \xe2\x80\x94 and learns to connect with them \xe2\x80\x94 she will have\na much better chance of winning the White House.\n\n\n\nSanders, the Vermont independent and the only self-described socialist in\nthe Senate, drew packed houses during a weekend barnstorming tour of Iowa.\nThe 2,500 people who attended his rally in Council Bluffs were believed to\nbe the largest crowd a candidate from either party has drawn in the state.\nThis followed last week\xe2\x80\x99s triumph in Madison, Wis. , where Sanders packed a\n10,000-seat arena with cheering supporters \xe2\x80\x94 the biggest event anywhere\nthus far in the campaign.\n\n\n\nAt the same time, Sanders is rising in the polls. The latest Quinnipiac\nsurvey showed Clinton with a 19-point lead in Iowa, 52 percent to 33\npercent. As recently as May, Clinton had a 45-point advantage.\n\n\n\nComparisons have been made to McCarthy, the Minnesota senator whose\nopposition to the Vietnam War galvanized support on college campuses and\nstunned the Democratic Party establishment. McCarthy\xe2\x80\x99s showing in the 1968\nNew Hampshire primary \xe2\x80\x94 he received 42 percent of the vote \xe2\x80\x94 helped lead\nincumbent Lyndon Johnson to pull out of the race.\n\n\n\nBut let\xe2\x80\x99s not get carried away. A lead of 19 points is a problem any\npolitician would love to have. Sanders\xe2\x80\x99s numbers had nowhere to go but up,\nand Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s nowhere but down. What\xe2\x80\x99s safe to say at present is that\nSanders \xe2\x80\x94 not Martin O\xe2\x80\x99Malley, Jim Webb or Lincoln Chafee \xe2\x80\x94 has become the\nnon-Clinton alternative for Democrats who, for whatever reason, are\nsuffering some Clinton fatigue.\n\n\n\nOne thing Sanders has going for himself is palpable authenticity. He is the\nantithesis of slick. To say there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing focus-grouped about the man is\nto understate; one doubts he knows what a focus group is. \xe2\x80\x9cRumpled\xe2\x80\x9d is the\nword most often used to describe him, but that\xe2\x80\x99s not quite right; it\xe2\x80\x99s not\nas if his suits are unpressed or his shirttails untucked. He\xe2\x80\x99s just all\nsubstance and no style \xe2\x80\x94 which, to say the least, makes him stand out among\npoliticians.\n\n\n\nClinton, by contrast, has always struggled to let voters see the\n\xe2\x80\x9cauthentic\xe2\x80\x9d her rather than the carefully curated, every-hair-in-place\nversion her campaigns have sought to project. Part of the problem, I\nbelieve, is that women in politics are held to an almost impossible\nstandard; no male candidate\xe2\x80\x99s wardrobe choice or tone of voice receives\nsuch microscopic scrutiny. But she also distances herself by campaigning as\nif she\xe2\x80\x99s protecting a big lead \xe2\x80\x94 which she is \xe2\x80\x94 and wants to avoid\noffending anyone. Last, when asked her favorite ice cream flavor, she\nreplied, \xe2\x80\x9cI like nearly everything.\xe2\x80\x9d What, vanilla lovers were going to\nabandon her if she had said chocolate?\n\n\n\nSanders\xe2\x80\x99s main appeal, however, is that he speaks unabashedly for the\nparty\xe2\x80\x99s activist left. He is witheringly critical of Wall Street, wants to\nbreak up the big banks, proposes single-payer health care and promises to\nraise taxes. He voted against the 2003 invasion of Iraq; Clinton, then a\nsenator, voted for it but now says that she made a mistake.\n\n\n\nEight years ago, Barack Obama made opposition to the Iraq war his signature\nissue and rode it to victory in Iowa and beyond. Will lightning strike the\nClinton machine twice?\n\n\n\nNot the same kind of lightning, surely, and not in the same manner. Obama\nis a uniquely gifted politician whose appeal went beyond the issues. He was\nable to make voters believe not just in him but also in themselves and\ntheir power to reshape the world. And as the first African American with a\nlegitimate chance to become president, he gave the nation a chance to make\nhistory.\n\n\n\nThis time, Clinton is the candidate with history on her side. The fact that\nshe could be the first woman elected president is not enough, by itself, to\nwin her the nomination. But it does matter. She, like Obama, offers voters\nthe chance to feel a sense of accomplishment.\n\n\n\nAnd nothing about Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s past remotely compares with the millstone of\nVietnam that weighed LBJ down and ultimately caused him to give up. I just\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t see a McCarthy scenario brewing \xe2\x80\x94 or an Obama scenario, either.\n\n\n\n*How Politico became a GOP stooge: Republicans want to destroy Hillary\nClinton\xe2\x80\x94and the media is helping them out\n<http://www.salon.com/2015/07/07/how_politico_became_a_gop_stooge_republicans_want_to_destroy_hillary_clinton%E2%80%94and_the_media_is_helping_them_out/>\n// Salon // Heather Digby Parton \xe2\x80\x93 July 7, 2015 *\n\n\n\nAaron Blake at The Washington Post wrote a piece yesterday entitled, \xe2\x80\x9cThe\nClinton campaign is roping off reporters. But who will cry for us?\xe2\x80\x9d in\nwhich he lamented the treatment the press received over the weekend at the\nhands of Hillary Clinton. Again. If you were to read the twitter feeds of\npolitical reporters as it unfolded, it was a trauma they will not soon\nforget.\n\n\n\nHere is Politico\xe2\x80\x99s Glenn Thrush, who saw the deeper meaning of the event\nimmediately:\n\n\n\nThe weirdly obsessive Clinton twitter stalker Maggie Haberman of the New\nYork Times brought all the strands of Clinton\xe2\x80\x99s various alleged crimes\ntogether:\n\n\n\nEven three days later, everyone\xe2\x80\x99s still abuzz:\n\n\n\nAaron Blake recounted the event in all its chilling detail and then rather\nsheepishly admitted that nobody in America really gives a damn about how\nHillary Clinton treats the press. (A point I made a month ago.) After all,\nthe press is held in only slightly higher esteem by the public than loan\nsharks and puppy mill operators.
